Praying on a Mat with Images: Condition on Prostration
Ruling on praying on an image-bearing mat provided one does not prostrate on the images

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying on a mat with animal images and apply the condition that prostration must not be on the images.

Core Idea
Images on a prayer mat are fine — just don't prostrate on them.

This lesson addresses a common situation: praying on a mat that has images of animals. Many assume such prayer is inherently disliked or invalid. The Hanafi school, relying on authentic hadith, permits it under one clear condition: the prostration spot must be free of images. Let's examine the ruling, its proof, and the reasoning behind the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage to find the overall ruling on praying on a mat with animal images. Look for keywords like 'لا تكره' (not disliked).
The ruling is 'mubah' (permissible) subject to a condition.
2
Pinpoint the condition
Find the phrase 'بشرط أن لا يسجد عليها'. This is the critical condition: the worshiper must not prostrate on the images.
The images may be at the standing or sitting places.
3
Locate the proof
The passage mentions 'ثبت ذلك عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الصحيحين'. Identify that this refers to authentic hadith (Bukhari and Muslim).
The hadith is about a cushion with images.
4
Understand the rationale
The passage explains why prostration on images is avoided: 'تشبه بعبادة الصنم' (resembles idol worship). This is the reason for the restriction.
The comparison emphasizes tawheed.
5
Apply the distinction
Now apply the ruling: if the prostration spot is clear, prayer is valid and permissible; if the image is under the forehead during prostration, it is makruh (disliked).
Other schools may have stricter views on images in the prayer area.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى بساط ذي صور لا يسجد عليها. قوله : وعلى بساط ؛ أي لا تكره الصلاة على بساط مصور بصورة الحيوان، بشرط أن لا يسجد عليها بأن تكون في موضع قيامه وجلوسه، فإنّ بسط البساط ذي الصور والاستناد على الوسادة التي فيها الصور جائز ) ، ثبت ذلك عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في صورة صغيرة لا تبدو للناظر، وتمثال غير حيوان. قوله : وعلى بساط ذي صور لا يسجد عليها. قوله : وعلى بساط ; أي لا تكره الصلاة على بساط مصور بصورة الحيوان، بشرط أن لا يسجد عليها بأن تكون في موضع قيامه وجلوسه، فإنّ بسط البساط ذي الصور والاستناد على الوسادة التي فيها الصور جائز ) ، ثبت ذلك عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الصحيحين وغيرهما فلا وجه لكراهة الصلاة فيه، نعم في السجدة عليها تشبه بعبادة الصنم فلذا وقع الاحتراز منه.
And on a mat with images upon which he does not prostrate. His statement: 'And on a mat' means: the prayer on a mat that has images of animals is not disliked, on condition that he does not prostrate on them, meaning the images are in the place where he stands and sits. Indeed, spreading a mat with images and leaning on a cushion that has images is permissible. This is established from the Prophet ﷺ in the two Sahihs and others, so there is no basis for disliking prayer in it; however, prostration on it resembles the worship of an idol, hence the restriction.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that praying on a mat with animal images is not disliked, provided that the worshiper does not prostrate directly on the images. The images should be at the place of standing or sitting. The proof is the authentic hadith of the Prophet ﷺ where he allowed a cushion with images. Prostration on images is avoided because it resembles idol worship. Thus the Hanafi position is that it is permissible to pray on such a mat as long as the prostration spot is free of images.

Key Terms
بساط ذي صور (bisat dhi suwar): A mat or carpet that has images (specifically animal images) depicted on it.
لا يسجد عليها (la yasjudu 'alayha): He does not prostrate on the images; i.e., the images should be in the place of standing or sitting, not where the forehead is placed during prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Such prayer is always disliked.

Key Takeaway
Praying on a mat with animal images is permissible as long as the prostration spot is free of images; prostration on images is disliked because it resembles idol worship.

Ruling on Small Images and Non-Animal Images in Prayer
Ruling on permissibility of small and non-animal images in prayer area

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on small images and non-animal images in the prayer area and identify the evidence (dalil) that supports it.

Core Idea
Small images not clearly visible and images of non-animals are permissible in prayer because they are not worshipped.

The presence of images during prayer is a matter of scholarly difference. The Hanafi school draws a distinction between images that are clearly visible and those that are not, as well as between images of living beings and non-living objects. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Hanafi permissibility of small or indistinct images and non-animal figures in the prayer space.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling categories
The passage rules on two distinct categories: (a) small images that are not clearly visible, and (b) images of non-animals (trees, houses, etc.). For both, the ruling is that they are not disliked (makruh) and do not affect the validity of prayer.
The key condition for small images is that the details of limbs are not distinguishable to a viewer standing from ground level.
2
Understand the rationale (wajh al-dalalah)
The rationale is that such images are not worshipped; they do not fall under the category of idols (awthan). The prohibition of images in Islamic law is tied to preventing idolatry. Therefore, images that are not treated as objects of worship are permissible.
This is based on the general maxim that the ruling follows the underlying cause (talil).
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The direct evidence cited is the practice of the Companions, e.g., Ibn Mas‘ud (رضي الله عنه) wearing a ring with a tree design. This shows that images of non-animals were permissible. For small images, the evidence is general practice and the absence of any explicit prohibition.
This is a dalil naqli (transmitted evidence) from Companions' actions, which serve as proof in Hanafi usul.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
Other madhhabs (e.g., Shafi‘i) generally consider any image of a living being as makruh, regardless of size. The Hanafi position is distinguished by its focus on the image's visibility and the underlying cause (worship).
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis permit small decorative images, while others may require removal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصورة صغيرة لا تبدو للناظر، وتمثال غير حيوان. قوله : وصورة ; أي لا تكره صورة صغيرة، بحيث لا تبدو ; أي لا تظهرُ للناظر من بعيد ; لأنَّ مثل ذلك لا يعبد، فلا يكون في حكم الوثن ، فلا يكره في البيت. قوله : صغيرة ; أي بحيث لا تتبيّن تفاصيل أعضائها للناظر قائماً وهي على الأرض. كذا قال الحلبي في «شرح المنية». قوله : غير حيوان ; كالشجر والبيوت ونحوها وجه عدم كراهته أنه ليس فيه تشبيهاً بعبادة الوثن، فلا يكره وضعه في البيت ولا الصلاة في بيت هو فيه، وإن كان أمامه أو فوقه.
And a small image that is not apparent to the viewer, and a non-animal figure. His statement: 'And an image' means: a small image that is not apparent, i.e., does not appear to the viewer from a distance, is not disliked, because such is not worshipped, so it is not in the category of an idol, hence it is not disliked in the house. His statement: 'Small' means: such that the details of its limbs are not distinguishable to a viewer standing while it is on the ground. Thus said al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munyah. His statement: 'Non-animal' like trees, houses, and the like; the reason for not disliking it is that it does not resemble the worship of an idol, so it is not disliked to place it in the house nor to pray in a house where it is, even if it is in front of him or above him.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage states two rulings: (1) A small image that is not clearly visible (e.g., details of limbs not discernible) is not disliked to have in the house or prayer area because it is not considered an idol. (2) Images of non-living things (trees, houses, etc.) are never disliked, as they bear no resemblance to idolatry. The proofs are the actions of the Companions, e.g., Ibn Mas‘ud’s ring with a tree design, and general principle that prohibition of images applies only to those that are worshipped. Thus the Hanafi position is that such images are permissible, and prayer is valid even if such images are present.

Key Terms
صورة صغيرة (surah saghirah): A small image whose details (e.g., limbs) are not distinguishable to a viewer standing from ground level.
لا تبدو للناظر (la tabdu li al-nazir): Not apparent to the viewer, meaning not clearly visible from a distance or without scrutiny.
تمثال غير حيوان (timthal ghayr hayawan): An image of a non-animal, such as trees, houses, or inanimate objects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following images is NOT considered disliked (makruh) in the prayer area according to the Hanafi school?
A) A large detailed painting of a horse.
B) A small carved tree on a ring.
C) A statue of a lion in front of the worshiper.
D) A photograph of a person above the place of prostration.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits small, indistinct images and non-animal images in the prayer area because they are not objects of worship.

Ruling on Small Images Not Visible to the Viewer
Clarifies the Hanafi position on small images in prayer with evidence and intra-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on small images in prayer and identify the key difference among Hanafi scholars regarding the definition of 'small'.

Core Idea
A small image whose details are not visible is not disliked; only prostrating directly on it is makruh.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi position on images in the prayer space. The key is that the ruling depends on the size and visibility of the image. We will examine the definitions provided by later Hanafi authorities, the evidence from the practice of the Companions, and the practical implications for prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon (presence of a small image in the prayer area) and its hukm level (mubah, not disliked).
The text clearly states 'لا يكره' (not disliked).
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the evidence cited: the practice of Companions having images on their rings, as recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq and other works.
The dalil is a Sunnah ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the evidence proves the ruling: small images are not treated as objects of worship, so they are not in the category of idols.
The Prophet's silent approval indicates permissibility.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe the different definitions of 'small': al-Qahistani (requires intense scrutiny), al-Muhit (not visible from afar), al-Khazanah (size of a bird or smaller).
Ibn Abidin prefers the definition that details are not discernible to a standing viewer.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
Determine which definition is relied upon for fatwa: Ibn Abidin's definition (details not discernible).
This is the most precise and widely accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«صحيح البخاري وغيره فلا وجه لكراهة الصلاة فيه، نعم في السجدة عليها تشبه بعبادة الصنم فلذا وقع الاحتراز منه.
قوله : وصورة ؛ أي لا تكره صورة صغيرة، بحيث لا تبدو ؛ أي لا تظهرُ للناظر من بعيد ؛ لأنَّ مثل ذلك لا يعبد، فلا يكون في حكم الوثن ، فلا يكره في البيت
قوله : صغيرة ؛ أي بحيث لا تتبيّن تفاصيل أعضائها للناظر قائماً وهي على الأرض. كذا قال الحلبي في «شرح المنية».
قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (1: 648): هذا أضبط مما في القهستاني حيث قال : بحيث لا تبدو للناظر إلا بتبصر بليغ كما في «الكرماني»، أو لا تبدو له من بعيد كما في «المحيط»، ثم قال : لكن في الخزانة : إن كانت الصورة مقدار طير يكره، وإن كانت أصغر فلا. وفي الهندية (1: 107): عن قاضي خان : الصورة الكبيرة التي تبدو للناظر من غير تكلف.
Sahih al-Bukhari and others: there is no basis for disliking prayer in it. Yes, prostrating on it resembles idol worship, so caution is taken. He says: 'and a picture' meaning a small picture is not disliked, such that it is not apparent, i.e., it does not appear to the viewer from afar, because such a thing is not worshipped, so it is not in the ruling of an idol, so it is not disliked in the house. He says: 'small' meaning that the details of its limbs are not distinguishable to the viewer standing while it is on the ground. Thus said al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munya. Ibn Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar (1:648): This is more precise than what is in al-Qahistani, who said: such that it does not appear to the viewer except with intense scrutiny as in al-Kirmani, or does not appear to him from afar as in al-Muhit. Then he said: but in al-Khazanah: if the picture is the size of a bird, it is disliked, and if smaller, then not. And in al-Hindiyya (1:107) from Qadi Khan: the large picture that appears to the viewer without effort.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the ruling on small images. The base text from al-Wiqaya states that a small image not visible to the viewer is not disliked. The commentator clarifies that 'small' means the details of its limbs are not discernible to a person standing nearby, as defined by al-Halabi. This definition is more precise than others who say it must be invisible from a distance or only visible with intense scrutiny. Ibn Abidin prefers the definition that details are not visible. There is also a view from al-Khazanah that an image the size of a bird is disliked, but smaller is not. The reasoning is that such small images are not treated as objects of worship (idols), so they are not disliked in the house or in prayer. However, the commentator notes that prostrating directly on such an image is disliked because it resembles idol worship. The evidence for permissibility includes the practice of the Companions who had images of trees on their rings, as recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq and other works.

Key Terms
صورة صغيرة (sura saghira): An image so small that its details are not visible to a viewer standing near it.
لا تبدو (la tabdu): Does not appear clearly to the viewer from a distance.
وثن (wathan): An idol; something worshipped besides Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a very small image (whose details are not visible) in the prayer area?
A) It is makruh tahrimi
B) It is mubah (not disliked)
C) It invalidates the prayer
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
A small image whose details are not visible is permissible in prayer; however, prostrating directly on it is disliked due to resemblance to idol worship.

Non-Animal Images in the Prayer Area Are Permissible
Explains that images of inanimate objects like trees are not disliked

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-animal images in the prayer area and identify the evidence from prophetic practice and companion usage.

Core Idea
Non-animal images in the prayer area are permissible because they do not resemble idol worship.

Images in the prayer space often raise concerns about reverence and distraction. The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction between images of living beings and those of inanimate objects. This lesson explores the ruling on non-animal images, showing that they are permissible based on explicit prophetic practice and the actions of the Companions. We will examine the textual evidence and understand the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on placing images of non-animal objects (trees, houses) in the prayer area. The ruling is that it is not disliked (ghayr makruh) and therefore permissible.
The opposite would be images of living beings, which have a different ruling.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Aisha, where the Prophet did not object to pillows with images after they were cut from a curtain. Also reports from Ibn Abbas and other Companions who permitted such images.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim, indicating strong transmission.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action of reclining on the pillows shows tacit approval. The Companions' use of rings with images of trees and animals further supports permissibility. The key point is that these images do not resemble objects of worship.
The rationale (illa) is the absence of resemblance to idol worship.
4
Note the consensus
The text indicates no disagreement within the Hanafi school on this point. It is considered muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) within the school, and the evidence is considered clear.
Other schools may have stricter views on images in general, but for non-animal images, there is broad agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : غير حيوان ؛ كالشجر والبيوت ونحوها وجه عدم كراهته أنه ليس فيه تشبيهاً بعبادة الوثن، فلا يكره وضعه في البيت ولا الصلاة في بيت هو فيه، وإن كان أمامه أو فوقه، وقد صح عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما عند الشيخين وغيرهما: أنه أجاز ذلك. وفي رواية: «أنها نصبت ستراً فيه تصاوير، فدخل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فنزعه فقطعته وسادتين، وكان رسول الله يرتفق عليهما» رواه البخاري ومسلم. وعن جابر رضي الله عنه قال: كان في خاتم ابن مسعود شجرة أو شيء بين ذبابين. وعن قتادة: كان نقش خاتم أبي موسى الأشعري أسد بين رجلين. وعن قتادة: كان نقش خاتم أنس بن مالك كركي أو طائر له رأسان. وعن القاسم: كان نقش خاتم شريح أسدان بينهما شجرة.
He says: 'non-animal' such as trees, houses, and the like. The reason for it not being disliked is that there is no resemblance to idol worship in it. So it is not disliked to place it in the house, nor to pray in a house where it is, even if it is in front of him or above him. It is authentically reported from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, in the Two Sahihs and others, that he permitted that. In a narration: 'She set up a curtain with images, then the Messenger of Allah entered and tore it, then she cut it into two pillows, and the Messenger of Allah used to recline on them' reported by Bukhari and Muslim. And from Jabir: 'In the ring of Ibn Mas'ud there was a tree or something between two flies.' And from Qatada: 'The engraving on the ring of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari was a lion between two men.' And from Qatada: 'The engraving on the ring of Anas ibn Malik was a crane or a bird with two heads.' And from al-Qasim: 'The engraving on the ring of Shurayh was two lions between them a tree.'
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for images of non-living things. The commentator explains that images of trees, houses, and similar objects are not disliked because they do not resemble idols. Therefore, it is permissible to have them in the house and to pray in a room containing such images, even if they are directly in front of or above the worshipper. The proof comes from multiple hadith: (1) The hadith of Aisha where the Prophet did not prohibit pillows with images after they were repurposed. (2) The hadith of Ibn Abbas permitting images of trees. (3) The practice of the Companions who had images of trees and animals on their rings, which shows that such small images were tolerated. This indicates that as long as the image is not an object of worship, it is permissible.

Key Terms
تمثال غير حيوان (timthal ghayr hayawan): An image or statue of something other than an animal, such as trees, buildings, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, is it permissible to pray in a room that has a picture of a tree on the wall?
A) Yes, it is permissible
B) No, it is makruh
C) No, it invalidates prayer
D) Yes, but only if the picture is small

Key Takeaway
Non-animal images are permissible in the prayer area because they do not involve resemblance to idols.

Ruling on Images of Animals with Head Removed
States the ruling on images of animals with erased heads and the proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an image of an animal is permissible (mubah) when its head is completely erased, and explain why a simple cut with a thread is insufficient.

Core Idea
An animal image becomes permissible only when its head is fully erased, because partial removal does not eliminate the resemblance to a living creature.

This lesson addresses a common issue in daily life: what to do with images of living beings that may be present during prayer. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance that simply cutting off the head with a thread may not be enough—the head must be thoroughly erased to remove the dislike (karahah). We will examine the proof from the hadith and the conditions that ensure the image is no longer considered an object of prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is possessing or depicting an image of a living animal. The ruling (hukm) is that it is permissible (mubah) if the head is erased; otherwise, it is disliked (makruh).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith from Sunan al-Nasa’i where Jibril (AS) instructs the Prophet (SAW) to cut off the heads of images on a curtain.
The hadith is a Sunnah ahad, so its transmission is zanni, but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace wajh al-dalalah
The imperative ‘cut off their heads’ indicates that removing the head eliminates the prohibition. The Hanafi school further derives that the cut must be effective in erasing the head completely.
4
Identify the conditions for removing dislike
The head must be completely obscured. Simply cutting with a thread is insufficient for animals with ringed necks (e.g., birds). Cutting off limbs alone does not remove the dislike.
The condition is derived from the word ‘muhya’ (erased), meaning the head must be fully hidden.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi‘i school allows cutting the head as long as it is removed, and also permits turning the image into a mat. The Hanafi school is more stringent in requiring full erasure.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on interpretation of the same hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو حيوان مُحيَ رأسه. قوله : محي ؛ أي قطع رأسه، يدلّ عليه ما في سنن النسائي: «استأذن جبريل على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال: ادخل، فقال: كيف أدخل وفي بيتك ستر فيه تصاوير؟ فإن كنت لا بد فاعلاً فاقطع رؤوسها، أو اجعلها بسطاً». ولو قطع رأسه بخيط ونحوه فهو لا ينفي الكراهة؛ لأن بعض الحيوانات مطوق فلا يتحقق قطعه إلا بمحوه، وهو بأن يجعل الخيط على كل رأسه بحيث يخفي، أو يطليه بطلاء ينفيه أو يغسله أو نحو ذلك، ولو قطع يديها ورجليها لا ترتفع الكراهة؛ لأن الإنسان قد تقطع أطرافه وهو حي. كذا في فتح القدير.
Or an animal whose head is erased. He says: 'erased' meaning its head is cut off. This is supported by what is in Sunan al-Nasa'i: 'Jibril asked permission to enter upon the Prophet, and he said: Enter. He said: How can I enter while there is a curtain with images in your house? If you must, then cut off their heads, or make them into mats.' And if he cuts its head with a thread or similar, it does not remove the dislike because some animals are ringed, so its cutting is not achieved except by erasing it, which is to place the thread over its entire head such that it conceals it, or to coat it with a substance that removes it, or to wash it, or similar. And if he cuts off its hands and feet, the dislike is not removed because a human may have his limbs cut off while alive. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage discusses how to properly render an animal image permissible. The base matn says 'an animal whose head is erased' (muhya ra'suhu). The commentator explains that 'erased' means the head is completely cut off. The proof is the hadith where Jibril advised the Prophet to cut off the heads of images on a curtain. However, simply cutting with a thread is not sufficient for animals with ringed necks (like birds) because the cut may not be clearly visible. The head must be fully obscured by the thread or painted over. Cutting off limbs alone does not remove the dislike, as a living person can have limbs amputated. The key is to eliminate the appearance of a living creature with a head. This ruling is from Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
محي (muhya): Having the head cut off or erased completely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required for an animal image to no longer be disliked?
A) Cut off its hands and feet
B) Remove its head completely
C) Cut its body into pieces
D) Turn it into a pillow

Key Takeaway
An image of an animal becomes permissible (mubah) in the Hanafi school only when its head is completely erased; simple cuts or limb removal are insufficient.

Ruling on Killing Snakes and Scorpions During Prayer
Provides the ruling for killing harmful creatures while praying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on killing harmful creatures during prayer and identify the condition that changes it from disliked to permissible.

Core Idea
Killing a snake or scorpion during prayer is allowed if you fear harm; otherwise it is disliked but does not break the prayer.

This lesson addresses a common concern: does killing a dangerous animal during prayer invalidate your salah? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the presence of fear. Understanding this ruling not only clarifies practical situations but also illustrates how a specific hadith permits actions that would normally be prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditional ruling
The act is killing a snake or scorpion during prayer. The ruling depends on whether the worshipper fears harm. If yes, it is not disliked (mubah). If no, it is disliked (makruh tanzih) but does not invalidate the prayer.
The key condition is 'khawf al-adha' (fear of harm).
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Iqtulu al-aswadayn fi al-salah' (Kill the two black ones in prayer) is the primary evidence. The explicit command indicates permission, and the phrase 'fi al-salah' confirms it applies during prayer.
The hadith is from the four Sunan (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah).
3
Analyze the reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
The command 'iqtulu' is understood as permission (ibaha) rather than obligation because of the context of harm. The concession overrides the general prohibition of excessive movement in prayer.
Hanafi usul: a command can indicate permission when there is evidence of concession.
4
Note the analogy (qiyas)
The commentator compares this to other concessions during prayer: warding off a passer-by and walking to renew wudu. These analogies show that necessary actions do not invalidate the prayer.
The common factor is the Lawgiver's permission (tarkhis).
5
Apply the ruling to similar creatures
By extension, any harmful creature (e.g., wasp, venomous spider) can be killed during prayer if there is genuine fear of harm. Without fear, it remains makruh tanzih.
The illa is harm (adha).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقتل الحية والعقرب في الصلاة. قوله : وقتل... الخ؛ أي لا يكره قتل الحية والعقرب في الصلاة؛ أي إذا خاف الأذى، وإلا يكره، لكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وإن احتاج إلى عمل كثير؛ لأن الشارع رخص فيه، فأشبه درء المارّ بين يديه، ومشي المحدث في الصلاة للوضوء، وذلك لحديث: «اقتلوا الأسودين في الصلاة» أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة.
And killing the snake and scorpion in prayer. He says: 'and killing... etc.' meaning that killing the snake and scorpion in prayer is not disliked, i.e., if he fears harm. Otherwise it is disliked, but it does not invalidate the prayer, even if it requires many actions. Because the Lawgiver has permitted it, so it resembles warding off a passer-by in front of him, and the walking of one who has broken wudu in prayer to perform wudu. And that is based on the hadith: 'Kill the two black ones in prayer' narrated by the authors of the four Sunan.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on killing harmful animals during prayer. The base matn mentions 'killing the snake and scorpion in prayer.' The commentator clarifies that if the worshipper fears harm from them, killing them is not disliked. If there is no fear, it is disliked (makruh tanzih) but does not invalidate the prayer, even if it involves many movements. This is because the Lawgiver has given a concession for it. The proof is the hadith: 'Kill the two black ones (i.e., the snake and scorpion) during prayer.' The ruling is analogized to other permissible actions during prayer like motioning to ward off a passer-by or walking to perform wudu.

Key Terms
قتل (qatl): Killing, specifically allowed in prayer for harmful creatures.
الأسودين (al-aswadayn): The two black ones: the snake and the scorpion as mentioned in the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a worshipper kills a scorpion during prayer out of fear of being stung?
A) It invalidates the prayer
B) It is not disliked
C) It is haram
D) It requires sujud al-sahw

Key Takeaway
Killing a harmful creature during prayer is permitted if there is fear of harm; otherwise it is disliked but does not invalidate the prayer.

Killing Snakes and Scorpions in Prayer: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on killing snakes/scorpions in prayer with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing snakes/scorpions in prayer, apply the distinction between fear of harm, and identify the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Killing a harmful snake or scorpion in prayer is permissible; much action does not invalidate because the Lawgiver granted a concession.

This lesson examines a specific concession within prayer: killing a snake or scorpion. While extensive movement normally invalidates prayer, the Lawgiver has made an exception for these dangerous creatures. We will explore the ruling, its conditions, and the textual evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing a snake or scorpion during prayer. The ruling is mubah (permissible) when fearing harm; makruh tanzihi otherwise.
The ruling depends on the presence of harm.
2
Understand the impact on prayer
The prayer remains valid even if much action is required, because the Lawgiver permitted it.
The concession overrides the general rule that much action invalidates.
3
Analyze the analogies
The commentator compares it to warding off a passerby or an impure person walking for wudu, which are also allowed despite extra movement.
These analogies show the principle of permitted extra motions in prayer.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'Kill the two black ones in prayer' is cited. In Hanafi usul, the imperative indicates permissibility (ibahah) in this context.
The hadith is specific and thus overrides general prohibitions of extra movement.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools hold that much action invalidates prayer; but Hanafis maintain the prayer is valid due to the specific concession.
The difference stems from how the general rule and specific permission are reconciled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : وقتل...الخ ؛ أي لا يكره قتل الحية والعقرب في الصلاة ؛ أي إذا خاف الأذى، وإلا يكره، لكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وإن احتاج إلى عمل كثير؛ لأنَّ الشارع رخص فيه، فأشبه درء المارّ بين يديه، ومشي المحدث في الصلاة للوضوء، وذلك لحديث : اقتلوا الأسودين في الصلاة ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة. كذا في النهاية»، و«البناية». حية، أو عقرب فيها ، والبول فوق بيت فيه مسجد) : أي مكان أعد للصلاة، وجُعِل له محراب، وإنما قلنا هذا لأنه لم يُغط له حُكْمُ المسجد. والله أعلم. النهاية»، و«البناية». [١] قوله : حَيّة ؛ هو بفتح الحاء المهملة وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية بالفارسية : مار.
[2] His saying: 'and killing...etc.' means it is not disliked to kill a snake or scorpion in prayer when one fears harm; otherwise it is disliked, but it does not invalidate the prayer even if it requires much action, because the Lawgiver permitted it. It resembles warding off a passerby in front of one or an impure person walking in prayer for ablution. This is based on the hadith: 'Kill the two black ones in prayer' recorded by the four Sunan compilers. Thus in al-Nihayah and al-Binayah. 'A snake or scorpion in it, and urinating on a roof above a place that has a mosque' meaning a place prepared for prayer with a mihrab. We only say this because it does not have the ruling of a mosque. And Allah knows best. Al-Nihayah and al-Binayah. [1] His saying: 'hayyah' it is with fatha on the ha and tashdid on the ya, in Persian: mar.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line regarding killing snakes and scorpions in prayer. The commentator explains that it is not disliked to kill them in prayer if one fears harm; otherwise it is disliked. However, even if much action is needed, the prayer is not invalidated because the Lawgiver has permitted it. This is analogous to warding off a passerby or a person with impurity walking to perform ablution. The proof is the hadith 'Kill the two black ones (snake and scorpion) in prayer.' The commentary also clarifies that the phrase 'in it' refers to a place prepared for prayer with a mihrab, which does not have the full ruling of a mosque. Finally, it gives the lexical definition of 'hayyah' as snake in Persian.

Key Terms
حية (ḥayyah): Snake, from the hadith about killing the two black ones.
أسودين (aswadayn): The two black ones: snake and scorpion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible and not disliked
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Invalidates the prayer
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
Killing a harmful snake or scorpion in prayer is permissible due to the hadith; the prayer remains valid even with much action.

Ruling on Urinating on a Roof Above a Musalla
Ruling on the permissibility of impurity on a roof above a musalla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing impurities on a roof above a prayer room and explain why it differs from a consecrated mosque.

Core Idea
A place prepared for prayer without waqf status is not a legal mosque, so acts like urination are not disliked on its roof.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding acts that would normally be prohibited in a mosque, such as urination, when done on a roof above a room designated for prayer. The commentary clarifies that this room is not a fully consecrated mosque but a musalla, which lacks the full sanctity and legal restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the subject
The passage addresses urination (and similar acts) on the roof of a house that contains a 'masjid'. Note the specific context: the house is privately owned.
2
Define 'masjid' in this context
The commentator specifies that 'masjid' here means a place prepared for sunnah and nawafil prayers, with a mihrab, cleaned and perfumed. It is not a waqf mosque.
The Arabic term 'musalla' is equivalent.
3
Understand the legal consequence
Because this room does not have the legal rulings of a mosque, there is no karahah (dislike) for performing impurities on its roof.
4
Recognize the reasoning
The passage states that such a place can be sold as part of the house, confirming it is not a waqf. A proper mosque cannot be sold.
5
Note the scope
This ruling is specific to a musalla inside a house. A dedicated congregational mosque would not permit such acts.

[٢]قوله : والبول ؛ أي لا يكره البول، وكذا الجماع والتغوط على سطح بيت فيه مسجد : أي موضع أعدّ للسنن والنوافل، بأن يُتخذ له محراب وينظف ويطيب، بل ولا يكره فيه أيضاً ؛ لأنه ليس له حكم المسجد شرعا ، فإنه يجوز بيعه ببيع البيت. [٣]قوله : هذا ؛ أي إنّما حكمنا بجواز هذا فيه وإنما فسرنا المسجد بهذا.
[2] His saying: 'and urinating' means it is not disliked to urinate, and likewise sexual intercourse and defecating on the roof of a house in which there is a mosque, i.e., a place prepared for sunnah and nawafil prayers, by having a mihrab made, cleaned, and perfumed. Rather, it is not disliked at all, because it does not have the legal ruling of a mosque; it is permissible to sell it with the sale of the house. [3] His saying: 'this' means we have only ruled its permissibility in this context, and we have interpreted 'mosque' in this way.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'wal-bawlu fawqa baytin fihi masjidun' (urinating on a roof above a house that has a mosque). The commentator clarifies that 'masjid' here refers to a place prepared for supererogatory prayers (sunnah and nawafil), equipped with a mihrab, cleaned, and perfumed. Such a place does not have the full legal status of a mosque; it can be sold as part of the house. Therefore, it is not disliked to urinate, have intercourse, or defecate on the roof above it. The final line emphasizes that the ruling is specific to this interpretation of 'masjid' as a musalla, not a congregational mosque.

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): In this context, a place prepared for sunnah and nafil prayers, with a mihrab, not a fully consecrated mosque.
بيت فيه مسجد (bayt fihi masjid): A house that contains a room set aside for prayer, not a public mosque.

Witr Prayer: Three Rak'at and Its Obligatory Status
Defining witr prayer and proving its obligation with hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of rak'at and legal classification of witr prayer, and identify the key hadith used to prove its obligation.

Core Idea
Witr is a wajib prayer of three rak'at, not a nafl, proven by a Prophetic hadith.

In this passage, the commentator elaborates on the chapter of witr and nafl prayers, clarifying that witr is not merely a supererogatory prayer but holds a higher rank: it is wajib. The text defines witr linguistically and legally, then provides the evidence from the sunnah. As you read, focus on how the Hanafi school derives the obligation (wujub) from the hadith and why witr is placed before nawafil in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its definition
Locate what is being defined: witr prayer. Note its linguistic meaning (odd number) and its legal application: the prayer performed after Isha. Also note the definition of nafl to contrast.
The author begins by establishing the scope: 'Chapter of witr and nafl'.
2
Extract the ruling and the number of rak'at
The text explicitly states 'الوتر ثلاث ركعات وجبت' – witr is three rak'at and wajib. Confirm the number of rak'at and the legal classification.
The ruling is given at the very beginning of the passage as a concise statement.
3
Locate the evidence for obligation
The commentator cites the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has supplied you with a prayer that is better for you than red camels, and it is witr, so He made it for you between Isha and Fajr.' This hadith is the primary proof for wujub.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.
4
Understand the Hanafi usul point
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form and the explicit making of a prayer indicate obligation (wujub). The hadith uses 'ja'ala' (made) which conveys assignment. Also, the hadith is ahad, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.
This distinction between fard and wajib is fundamental: fard is from qat'i evidence, wajib from zanni.
5
Contrast with nawafil and note the hierarchy
The passage explains that witr is not a nafl; it is wajib. The author placed it before nawafil because it is superior. Nafl includes sunnah and mustahabb, but witr is above them.
This ordering is pedagogical: witr is treated first due to its higher legal status.

Source Text

Chapter of witr and nafl prayers. The witr is three rak'at, wajib. [1] His saying: 'Chapter of witr...' etc.; that is, this is a chapter on the rulings of the prayer known as witr and nafl. Witr, with kasr of the waw and also with fatha, and the first is more famous, meaning odd number, opposite of even. When used in the chapter of prayers, what is intended is the witr performed after Isha. Nafl: linguistically, the extra; and legally, it applies to a prayer that is neither fard nor wajib, inclusive of whether it is a confirmed sunnah or a recommended act. Since witr is superior to nafl prayers because it is wajib, the author mentioned it first, and he did not discuss the sunan prayers, even though they are mentioned in this chapter, because they are included in nafl. [2] His saying: 'three rak'at' — that is, like Maghrib prayer, because al-Tahawi and al-Bayhaqi and others narrated that Ibn Umar was asked about witr and said: Do you know the witr of the day? He said: Maghrib prayer. He said: Likewise the witr of the night. Indeed, and al-Hakim and others narrated that the Prophet ﷺ used to pray witr with three, not making taslim except at the end. And in Sahih al-Bukhari and others are narrations that indicate three. [3] His saying: 'wajib' — due to the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has supplied you with a prayer that is better for you than red camels, and that is witr, so He made it for you between Isha and Fajr.' related by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the chapter on witr and nafl prayers. It begins by defining witr linguistically and legally. Legally, witr is a prayer performed after Isha, consisting of three rak'at, and it is wajib (obligatory) according to the Hanafi school. The commentator clarifies that witr is not a nafl; nafl includes all voluntary prayers, but witr is wajib and thus superior. The author (al-Quduri or al-Marghinani) placed it before nawafil because of its higher status. The passage then provides hadith evidence: a narration from Ibn Umar comparing witr to Maghrib in format (three rak'at with one taslim at the end), and a specific hadith indicating that witr is obligatory ('wajibat') and is better than red camels. The commentator notes that these proofs establish the obligation. The commentary also notes that the author did not separately list the sunan prayers because they fall under nawafil.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after Isha, consisting of three rak'at, and considered wajib in the Hanafi school.
نافلة (nafilah): Any supererogatory prayer that is neither fard nor wajib, including sunnah mu'akkadah and mustahabb.
وجبت (wajibat): This text means witr is obligatory (wajib), not merely recommended.

Status of Witr Prayer: Wajib or Sunnah?
Dispute over the legal status of Witr prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on Witr prayer, identify the evidence for its wujub, and evaluate the counter-arguments from the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib in the Hanafi school due to ahad hadiths indicating obligation, distinct from fard which requires qat'i evidence.

The status of Witr prayer has been a subject of scholarly debate. While most schools consider it a highly recommended sunnah, the Hanafi school, following Imam Abu Hanifa, classifies it as wajib (obligatory). This lesson examines the evidence and reasoning behind this difference, focusing on the key hadiths cited and the principles of hadith interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its evidence
Read the passage to find what Abu Hanifa says about Witr and which hadiths he uses to prove obligation.
The hadiths are: 'Indeed Allah has provided you with a prayer better than red camels, it is Witr…' and 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray Witr is not of us.'
2
Identify the opposing position and its evidence
Find what the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i hold and which hadith they cite.
Their hadith: 'Three things are obligatory upon me and supererogatory for you: Witr, sacrifice, and Duha prayer.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing evidence
Understand how the Hanafis rebut the opposing hadith. They claim it was before the final ruling on Witr was established.
Al-Tahawi argued that the hadiths indicating obligation are later and abrogate the earlier ones.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider how this difference in ruling affects actual practice: in the Hanafi school, missing Witr is sinful; in others, it is not.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi
Recognize that even within the Hanafi school there are different narrations, but the famous (mufta bihi) position is that Witr is wajib.
The text mentions three narrations from Abu Hanifa: wajib, fard, and sunnah. The 'famous' one is wajib.

الوتر ثلاث ركعات وجبت

نافلة: لغة: الزائدة، ويطلق شرعاً على صلاة ليست بفرض ولا بواجبة، أعم من أن تكون سنة مؤكدة او مستحباً، ولما كان الوتر أفضل من النوافل؛ لكونه واجباً، قدمه المصنّف ذكراً، ولم يتعرّض لذكر السنن، مع أنها مذكورة في هذا الباب؛ لدخولها في النوافل.

هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما وعند الشَّافِعِي فهو سنة
قوله: عند أبي حنيفة له؛ أي في إحدى الروايات الثلاثة عنه، وهي المشهورة، وفي رواية: إنّه فرض، وفي رواية: سنّة.
قوله: فهو سنّة؛ لحديث: «ثلاث هنّ علي فرائض، وهنّ لكم تطوّع، الوتر والنحر وصلاة الضحى»؛ لحديث أخرجه أحمد والحاكم وغيرهما، وهناك أخبار أخر أيضاً تدلّ على كونه تطوّعاً، وأجاب الطحاوي وغيره عنها بأنها قبل استقرار أمر الوتر بدليل ورود الأحاديث الدالة على الوجوب.
قوله: خلافاً للشافعي؛ أي في أحد أقواله الثلاثة: أحدها: كقولنا،
Chapter of Witr and Nawafil Prayer. Witr is three rak'ahs, it became obligatory. ... His saying: Chapter of Witr etc.: meaning this is a chapter on the rulings of prayers known as Witr and Nawafil. Witr (with kasra on waw and fatha, the first is more famous) is the opposite of even (shaf'), and when used absolutely in the chapter of prayers, what is meant is the Witr performed after Isha. Nawafil: linguistically: extra; legally: it is applied to a prayer that is neither fard nor wajib, encompassing whether it is a confirmed sunnah or recommended. Since Witr is superior to nawafil because it is wajib, the author mentioned it first, and did not address the sunan, even though they are mentioned in this chapter, because they are included in nawafil. His saying: three rak'ahs: like the Maghrib prayer, as narrated by al-Tahawi and al-Bayhaqi and others: 'Ibn Umar was asked about Witr and he said: Do you know the witr of the day? He said: Maghrib prayer. He said: Likewise the witr of the night.' And al-Hakim and others narrated: 'The Prophet used to pray Witr with three, not giving salutation except at the end.' His saying: it became obligatory: due to the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has provided you with a prayer that is better for you than red camels, and it is Witr, and He made it for you between Isha and Fajr.' Reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah and others. And Abu Dawud narrated and al-Hakim authenticated it as marfu': 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray Witr is not of us.' This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i, it is sunnah. His saying: according to Abu Hanifa: i.e., in one of the three narrations from him, and it is the famous one; in one narration it is fard, and in one narration it is sunnah. His saying: it is sunnah: due to the hadith: 'Three things are obligatory upon me and supererogatory for you: Witr, the sacrifice (al-nahr), and Salat al-Duha.' Reported by Ahmad and al-Hakim and others, and there are other reports indicating it is supererogatory. Al-Tahawi and others answered these by saying they were before the stabilization of the matter of Witr, evidenced by the hadiths indicating obligation. His saying: contrary to al-Shafi'i: i.e., in one of his three sayings: one of them is like our saying.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with the chapter of Witr and Nawafil prayers. The author begins by defining key terms. 'Witr' (with kasra or fatha on the waw, the former being more famous) means odd, opposite of even (shaf'). In the context of prayers, it specifically refers to the Witr prayer performed after Isha. 'Nafilah' (pl. nawafil) linguistically means 'extra', and legally refers to a prayer that is neither fard nor wajib, encompassing both confirmed sunnah and mustahabb. The author mentions Witr first because it is superior to other nawafil due to its being wajib. The matn states that Witr is three rak'ahs and 'wajabat' (it became obligatory or it is obligatory). The commentator provides evidence. First, it is like Maghrib in number, narrated from Ibn Umar. Second, a hadith that the Prophet ﷺ prayed Witr with three rak'ahs and only gave salutation at the end. The ruling of obligation is supported by the hadith: 'Allah has provided you with a prayer better than red camels, it is Witr, between Isha and Fajr.' Another hadith says 'Witr is a right; whoever does not pray it is not of us.' Then the key intra- and inter-madhhab dispute is presented. According to Abu Hanifa, in his famous narration, Witr is wajib. There are also narrations that it is fard or sunnah. According to his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i, it is sunnah. Their evidence includes the hadith: 'Three things are obligatory upon me and supererogatory for you: Witr, sacrifice, and Duha prayer.' Al-Tahawi and others responded that these hadiths were before the final ruling on Witr was settled, and they are superseded by the hadiths indicating obligation. The commentator notes that al-Shafi'i has three sayings on this, one of which agrees with the Hanafi position. This passage illustrates how the Hanafi school derives the ruling of Witr as wajib (obligatory but not at the level of fard) based on ahad hadiths, while other schools consider it sunnah.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after Isha, consisting of three rak'ahs.
النوافل (al-nawafil): Plural of nafilah; prayers that are neither fard nor wajib, including both sunnah mu'akkadah and mustahabb.
وجبت (wajabat): It became obligatory; in this context indicating the ruling of wujub according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sunnah
B) Wajib
C) Fard
D) Mustahabb

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers Witr wajib based on ahad hadiths, distinguishing it from fard which requires qat'i evidence.

The Legal Status of Witr: Wajib or Sunnah?
Intra-Hanafi and Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on Witr's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Witr, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and evaluate the proofs for wujub.

Core Idea
Witr is wajib in the Hanafi school — a right (haqq) that must be fulfilled, but not fard because the proof is zanni.

The Witr prayer is a nightly act of worship with a rich legal history. While all schools agree on its importance, its legal classification differs. This lesson examines the Hanafi position—including the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two main students—and the proofs that settled the issue for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three narrations from Abu Hanifa
Note that the passage mentions three narrations from Abu Hanifa: fard, wajib (the well-known), and sunnah. Understand the range of opinions.
The well-known narration (mashhura) is that Witr is wajib.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that Witr is sunnah mu'akkadah, aligning with al-Shafi'i's view.
This creates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The hadith 'al-witr haqq' is cited. In Hanafi usul, 'haqq' indicates wujub. Understand how this establishes obligation.
The hadith is ahad, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.
4
Examine the opposing proof
The hadith 'Three are obligatory upon me and voluntary for you' is used by Sahibayn and Shafi'i to argue Witr is sunnah.
The phrase 'lakum tatawwu' suggests it is supererogatory for the community.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
Al-Tahawi and others argue that the hadith about 'three things' came before Witr's obligation was settled. Later hadiths like 'al-witr haqq' established wujub.
This tafsir of the temporal priority resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة صلاة الوتر والنوافل بسلام: أي بسلام واحد خلافاً للشافعي. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما وعند الشافعي فهو سنة. وأخرج أبو داود وصححه الحاكم مرفوعاً: «الوتر حق، من لم يوتر فليس منا».

[١] قوله: عند أبي حنيفة له؛ أي في إحدى الروايات الثلاثة عنه، وهي المشهورة، وفي رواية: إنّه فرض، وفي رواية: سنّة.

[٢] قوله: فهو سنّة؛ لحديث: «ثلاث هنّ علي فرائض، وهنّ لكم تطوّع: الوتر والنحر وصلاة الضحى»؛ لحديث أخرجه أحمد والحاكم وغيرهما، وهناك أخبار أخر أيضاً تدلّ على كونه تطوّعاً، وأجاب الطحاوي وغيره عنها بأنها قبل استقرار أمر الوتر بدليل ورود الأحاديث الدالة على الوجوب.

[٤] في سنن أبي داود (٢) : (٦٢) ، و(المستدرك (١ : ٤٤٨)، وصححه، وحسنه ابن الهمام والتهانوي في (إعلاء السنن» (٦: ٣).
The Book of Prayer: The Witr and supererogatory prayers with one salutation: meaning with one salutation, contrary to al-Shafi'i. This is according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i, it is Sunnah. And Abu Dawood narrated and al-Hakim authenticated it as raised: 'The Witr is a right; whoever does not perform Witr is not of us.'

[1] His statement: 'according to Abu Hanifa' – i.e., in one of the three narrations from him, which is the well-known one. In one narration: it is fard. In one narration: it is Sunnah.

[2] His statement: 'it is Sunnah' – due to the hadith: 'Three are obligatory upon me and voluntary for you: Witr, sacrifice, and Duha prayer' – a hadith narrated by Ahmad, al-Hakim, and others. There are also other reports indicating it is voluntary. Al-Tahawi and others responded that these were before the establishment of the Witr command, evidenced by the hadiths indicating obligation.

[4] In Sunan Abi Dawud (2): 62, and al-Mustadrak (1:448), and he authenticated it, and Ibn al-Humam and al-Tahanawi deemed it good in I'la' al-Sunan (6:3).
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi and Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over the legal status of Salat al-Witr. The main text states that according to Abu Hanifa, Witr is performed with one salutation (i.e., three rak'ahs with one tashahhud and salam), while according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and al-Shafi'i, it is Sunnah. The commentary then explains that for Abu Hanifa, there are three narrations: the well-known one that Witr is wajib (obligatory but lesser than fard), another that it is fard (which is less accepted), and another that it is Sunnah. For the Sahibayn, the evidence is a hadith where the Prophet said certain acts are obligatory for him but voluntary for the community, including Witr. Al-Tahawi reconciles this by stating that those hadiths came before the final obligation was settled. The hadith from Abu Dawud (''al-witr haqq'') supports the obligation. Thus, the Hanafi position settled on Witr being wajib (as per Zahir al-Riwayah), not fard, because the proofs are from ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut).

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after the night prayer, consisting of three rak'ahs (in the Hanafi school).
بسلام (bi-salam): With one salutation (tashahhud and salam) at the end of the three rak'ahs.
حق (haqq): A right; in this context, it indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the legal status of Salat al-Witr?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules Witr as wajib (obligatory but sub-fard) based on the hadith 'al-witr haqq', and the mufta bihi position requires making it up if missed.

The Timing and Manner of Qunut in Witr
Hanafi rule on qunut before ruku and its wording, with Shafi'i difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school recites qunut in Witr before ruku and evaluate the Shafi'i argument for after ruku.

Core Idea
Qunut in Witr is before ruku in the Hanafi school; Shafi'i after-ruku reports refer to Fajr calamity qunut.

The qunut supplication in Witr is a well-known practice, but its timing differs between madhhabs. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position that qunut is recited before bowing (ruku) in the last rak'ah, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that it comes after ruku. We will trace the proof texts and understand the interpretive principles that lead to the Hanafi conclusion.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: qunut is recited before the ruku of the third rak'ah, the takbir is said with raising hands, and the recitation is preferably silent.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds qunut in Witr is recited after ruku, and also has a separate qunut in Fajr for calamities before ruku.
3
Examine the proofs
Hanafi relies on hadith in Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah showing the Prophet performed qunut before ruku. The Shafi'i uses hadith in Sahih Muslim showing qunut after ruku.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the after-ruku report as referring to the Fajr calamity qunut, not the regular Witr qunut. The consistent practice for Witr is before ruku.
5
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis raise hands for takbir before qunut and recite silently; Shafi'is in Witr do not raise hands (in the after-ruku position) and recite aloud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقنت قبل ركوع الثالثة، يكبر رافعاً يديه، ثم يقنت فيه أبداً خلافاً للشافعي فإن القنوت عنده بعد الركوع.

[١] قوله: ويقنت؛ بضم النون: أي يقرأ دعاء: وهو اللهم إنا نستعينك.... الخ، الذي يقرؤه أصحابنا الحنفية، أو اللهم اهدني في من هديت... الذي يقرؤه أصحاب الشافعي في الفجر، والأحب أن يجمع بينهما. ومن لا يحفظ الدعاء المأثور يقرأ: {رَبَّنَا آتِنَا فِي الدُّنْيَا حَسَنَةً وَفِي الْآخِرَةِ حَسَنَةً وَقِنَا عَذَابَ النَّارِ}، وقيل يقول: اللهم اغفر لي يكررها ثلاثاً، وقيل: يا رب يكرّر ثلاثاً كذا في «الذخيرة»، وهل يقرأ جهراً أو إخفاء؟ الكل واسع، والمختار هو الثاني.

[٢] قوله: قبل ركوع الثالثة؛ وهو المنقول من فعله في «سنن النسائي» وابن ماجة، وورد في «صحيح مسلم وغيره قنوته قبل الركوع أيضاً، وبه أخذ الشافعي عندنا محمول على قنوت الفجر للنوازل.
And he performs qunut before the ruku of the third (rak'ah), he says takbir raising his hands, then he performs qunut in it always, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for qunut according to him is after ruku.

[1] His statement: 'and he performs qunut' – with a dammah on the nun: meaning he recites the supplication: 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka...' etc., which our Hanafi scholars recite, or 'Allahumma ihdini fiman hadayta...' which the Shafi'i scholars recite in Fajr, and it is preferred to combine both. And whoever does not memorize the transmitted supplication recites: 'Our Lord, give us in this world good and in the Hereafter good and protect us from the punishment of the Fire.' And it is said he says: 'Allahumma ighfir li' repeating it three times, and it is said: 'Ya Rabb' repeating it three times, as in al-Dhakhirah. And is it recited aloud or silently? All are permissible, and the chosen is the second (i.e., silently).

[2] His statement: 'before the ruku of the third' – and this is transmitted from his action in Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah, and it is reported in Sahih Muslim and others that he performed qunut before ruku as well, and this is what al-Shafi'i adopted. According to us, it is interpreted as the qunut of Fajr for calamities.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage details the Hanafi practice of qunut (supplication) in Witr. According to the Hanafi school, qunut is recited before the ruku of the third rak'ah. The worshipper raises his hands during the takbir (which is done before qunut) and then recites the qunut. This is contrary to the Shafi'i school, where qunut in Witr is recited after rising from ruku. The commentary then explains the wording of qunut: the Hanafi supplication begins with 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka', while the Shafi'i supplication for Fajr begins with 'Allahumma ihdini'. It is recommended (mustahab) to combine both. For those who do not know these, they may recite the Quranic verse (2:201) or simply say 'Allahumma ighfir li' or 'Ya Rabb' three times. The issue of reciting aloud (jahr) or silently (ikhfa') is discussed: both are permissible, but the preferred view is silent recitation. The evidence for qunut before ruku comes from the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) narrated in Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah. The reports in Sahih Muslim about qunut after ruku are interpreted as referring to the qunut of Fajr for calamities, not the regular Witr qunut.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited in the Witr prayer (and occasionally in Fajr) asking for guidance, forgiveness, etc.
يكبر رافعاً يديه (yukabbiru rafi'an yadayhi): He says Allahu Akbar raising his hands (to the ears or shoulders) before beginning the qunut.
جهراً أو إخفاء (jahran aw ikhfa'an): Reciting qunut aloud or silently. The preferred view is silently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Before the ruku of the third rak'ah
C) After the second rak'ah
D) At the end of the prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recites qunut in Witr before ruku, relying on specific hadith, and interprets the after-ruku reports as referring to Fajr calamity qunut.

Qunut in Witr: Hanafi Position and Shafi'i Dispute
Explains the ruling of qunut in witr with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qunut in witr (timing, duration, and supplication) and explain the shar'i basis for the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Qunut in witr is a confirmed sunnah before ruku throughout the year, contrary to the Shafi'i position of after ruku and only in Ramadan.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a well-known difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the qunut in witr prayer. The Hanafi school holds that qunut is performed before ruku in the third rak'ah and is recited in every witr prayer throughout the year. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, places qunut after ruku and limits it to the latter half of Ramadan. Both schools support their positions with hadith evidence. This lesson will walk you through the ruling, the proofs, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the exact ruling: qunut is before ruku in the third rak'ah, with raising hands, and it is performed in every witr prayer (not just Ramadan).
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds qunut after ruku and only in the latter half of Ramadan. Also note his view on qunut in Fajr as a regular sunnah.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Look at the hadith from Ubayy ibn Ka'b in Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah showing the Prophet performed qunut before ruku, and the teaching hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud indicating it is for all witr prayers.
The teaching hadith is general and not restricted to Ramadan.
4
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Consider the hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud that Ubayy ibn Ka'b only performed qunut in the latter half of Ramadan, and the hadiths about qunut after ruku in Fajr and witr.
Hanafis interpret these as specific to calamities (nawazil) or as a practice later abrogated.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi school reconciles the evidence: the general teaching hadith takes precedence over the specific practice of Ubayy, and the qunut after ruku is interpreted as qunut of Fajr for calamities. The Fajr qunut was only for a month and then abandoned.
Ibn al-Qayyim's verification in Zad al-Ma'ad is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقنت قبل ركوع الثالثة ، يكبر رافعاً يديه ، ثُمَّ يقنتُ فيه أبداً (ويقنت قبل ركوع الثالثة)، خلافاً للشَّافِعِي ) له فإنَّ القُنُوتَ عنده بعد الركوع ، يكبِّرُ رافعاً يديه ، ثُمَّ يقنتُ فيه أبداً) خلافاً للشافعي (1) وثانيهما: الوتر ثلاثاً بتسليمتين، بأن يتشهد على رأس الركعتين ويُسلّم ثم يصلي ركعة واحدة، وثالثهما : أنّه مخيّر بين أن يوتر بركعة وبين أن يوتر بثلاث بتسلمية.
[ ۱ ] قوله : ويقنت ؛ بضم النون : أي يقرأ دعاء : وهو اللهم إنا نستعينك.... الخ، الذي يقرؤه أصحابنا الحنفية، أو اللهم اهدني فيمن هديت... الذي يقرؤه أصحابُ الشافعي له في الفجر، والأحب أن يجمع بينهما.
ومن لا يحفظ الدعاء المأثور يقرأ : وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَقُولُ رَبَّنَاءَ ايْنَا فِي الدُّنْيَا حَسَنَةً وَفِي الْآخِرَةِ حَسَنَةً وَقِنَا عَذَابَ النَّارِ ) ، وقيل يقول : اللهم اغفر لي يكررها ثلاثاً ، وقيل : يا رب يكرّر ثلاثاً كذا في «الذخيرة»، وهل يقرأ جهراً أو إخفاء الكل واسع، والمختار هو الثاني.
[٢] قوله : قبل ركوع الثالثة ؛ وهو المنقول من فعله ه ) في «سنن النسائي» وابن ماجة، وورد في «صحيح مسلم وغيره قنوته قبل الركوع أيضاً) وبه أخذ الشافعي له عندنا محمول على قنوت الفجر للنوازل.
الصلاة صلاة الوتر والنوافل
فإنَّ قُنوت الوتر عنده في النصف الأخير من رمضان فقط، دون غيره)، خلافاً للشَّافِعِي (١) له في الفجر"
[١] قوله : فقط ؛ لما أخرجه أبو داود : «إنّ أبي بن كعب له كـان يـؤمهم في التراويح، ويقنت في النصف الآخر من رمضان فقط ) ، ولنا : حديث تعليم النبي القنوت المروي في سنن أبي داود وغيره من الأحاديث الدالة على كونه في جميع السنة (٣)
[٢] قوله : في الفجر ؛ فعنده السُنَّة أن يقنت في صلاة الفجر بعد ركوع الثانية ؛ لما روي أن النبي كان يفعل ذلك (4) ، أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم. ولنا ما روي أنه قنت شهراً يدعو على قبائل من الكفار، ثم ترك) ، أخرجه مسلم وغيره، والأحاديث الواردة في قنوته في الفجر وغيره كلها محمولة على قنوت النوازل ونحو ذلك ، كما حققه ابن القيم في زاد المعاد في هدي خير العباد».
He performs qunut before the ruku of the third [rak'ah], he says takbir raising his hands, then he performs qunut in it always. (He performs qunut before the ruku of the third), contrary to al-Shafi'i. For him, qunut is after ruku. He says takbir raising his hands, then he performs qunut in it always. Contrary to al-Shafi'i. The second [opinion]: witr is three with two taslims, by sitting for tashahhud at the end of two rak'ahs and saying taslim, then praying one rak'ah. The third: that he is given a choice between performing witr as one rak'ah or as three with one taslim. [Footnote 1]: His saying: 'and he performs qunut' – with dammah on the nun: meaning he recites a supplication: 'O Allah, we seek Your help...' etc., which our Hanafi companions recite, or 'O Allah, guide me among those You have guided...' which al-Shafi'i's companions recite in Fajr. It is preferred to combine both. And whoever does not know the transmitted supplication recites: 'And among them are those who say, Our Lord, give us in this world good and in the Hereafter good and protect us from the punishment of the Fire.' Some say: he says 'O Allah, forgive me' repeating it three times. Some say: 'O Lord' repeating three times. Thus in al-Dhakhira. And whether he recites aloud or silently, all is permissible, and the chosen is the second. [Footnote 2]: His saying: 'before the ruku of the third' – and that is what is transmitted from his action in Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah, and it is reported in Sahih Muslim and others that he performed qunut before ruku as well. And al-Shafi'i adopted this. According to us, it is interpreted as qunut of Fajr for calamities. Prayer - Prayer of Witr and Nawafil Then: Indeed, his qunut of witr is only in the latter half of Ramadan, not otherwise, contrary to al-Shafi'i in Fajr. [Footnote 1]: His saying: 'only' – because of what Abu Dawud narrated: 'Ubayy ibn Ka'b used to lead them in tarawih and would perform qunut only in the latter half of Ramadan.' And our proof is the hadith teaching the Prophet's qunut narrated in Sunan Abi Dawud and other hadiths indicating that it is throughout the whole year. [Footnote 2]: His saying: 'in Fajr' – for him the sunnah is to perform qunut in Fajr prayer after the ruku of the second rak'ah, because it is narrated that the Prophet used to do that, narrated by Muslim, the authors of the Sunan, and others. And our proof is that it is narrated that he performed qunut for a month supplicating against tribes of disbelievers, then he left it, narrated by Muslim and others. And the hadiths about his qunut in Fajr and other prayers are all interpreted as qunut for calamities and the like, as verified by Ibn al-Qayyim in Zad al-Ma'ad fi Hady Khayr al-'Ibad.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the qunut in witr prayer. It begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: qunut is performed before ruku in the third rak'ah, with raising hands, and it is recited always (meaning in every witr prayer of the year). The author then notes the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who places qunut after ruku. Additionally, the text mentions two other opinions about the form of witr: one with two taslims (two rak'ahs then one) and another with one taslim (three rak'ahs together). The commentary then explains the content of the qunut supplication, including the Hanafi and Shafi'i versions, and recommends combining them. It also addresses what to recite if one does not know the transmitted du'a, citing Quranic verses and simple phrases. Regarding the timing, the commentary provides hadith evidence from Sunan al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah that the Prophet performed qunut before ruku, and also notes that the hadith of qunut after ruku is interpreted as qunut of Fajr for calamities. The main dispute about the duration of qunut in witr is then addressed: the Shafi'i position limits it to the latter half of Ramadan, while the Hanafi position holds it is throughout the year, supported by the hadith of teaching the qunut supplication. Finally, the commentary addresses the qunut in Fajr prayer, explaining that Shafi'is consider it a sunnah, while Hanafis view it as only for calamities (nawazil). All hadiths about qunut in Fajr are interpreted as such, as verified by Ibn al-Qayyim.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): A specific supplication recited in witr prayer (and in Fajr for calamities) while standing, either before or after ruku.
الوتر (al-witr): An odd-numbered prayer (usually 3 rak'ahs) performed after the night prayer (`Isha), considered a sunnah mu'akkadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds qunut in witr before ruku throughout the year, supported by the Prophet's general teaching, while the Shafi'i school limits it to the latter half of Ramadan after ruku, a difference rooted in interpreting specific hadiths.

Recitation in Each Rak'ah of Witr
States the ruling on recitation in witr: Fatiha and a surah in every rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in witr prayer and identify the sunnah practice regarding surah selection.

Core Idea
Reciting a surah after al-Fatiha in witr is sunnah, not obligatory, and any surah suffices.

Witr prayer has specific recitation guidelines. In every rak'ah, al-Fatiha is recited, and it is sunnah to add a surah. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the additional surah is not obligatory, but following the Prophet's practice is recommended. This lesson clarifies the ruling and the recommended practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is recitation in each rak'ah of witr prayer. The ruling: al-Fatiha must be recited, and a surah is recited as a sunnah.
The matn states 'يقرأ في كل ركعة منه الفاتحة وسورة'.
2
Distinguish between wajib and sunnah
In the Hanafi school, reciting al-Fatiha is wajib (obligatory but not fard), while reciting a surah after it is sunnah. Omission of the surah does not invalidate the prayer.
This distinction affects the validity: if al-Fatiha is omitted, the prayer is invalid; if a surah is omitted, the prayer is valid but the sunnah is missed.
3
Note the permissibility of any surah
The commentary explains 'أي أي سورة شاء' – any surah the worshiper wishes is allowed. There is no restriction to specific surahs.
This refutes the misconception that a particular surah must be recited.
4
Recognize the preference for following the Prophet's practice
Though any surah is permissible, it is preferable (الأولى) to follow what is established from the Prophet (peace be upon him). The text mentions he would recite Surah al-A'la in the first rak'ah.
The complete recommended set for witr includes al-A'la, al-Kafirun, and al-Ikhlas, but the source only mentions the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقرأ في كل ركعة منه الفاتحة، وسورة
[٣] قوله : وسورة ؛ أي أي سورة شاء، والأولى الاتباع بما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وكان يقرأ تارة في الركعة الأولى من الوتر بـ سبح اسم ربك الأعلى وفي
And he recites in every rak'ah of it al-Fatiha and a surah. [Footnote 3]: His saying: 'and a surah' – meaning any surah he wishes, and it is preferable to follow what is established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). And he would sometimes recite in the first rak'ah of witr [Surah] al-A'la (Sabbih isma rabbika al-a'la) and in...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This is a concise ruling statement about the recitation in witr prayer. The matn simply says that in each rak'ah of witr, one recites al-Fatiha and a surah. The commentary expands on 'surah' meaning any surah one chooses, but it is better to follow the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him), who would recite specific surahs: sometimes in the first rak'ah Surah al-A'la (Sabbih isma rabbika al-a'la). The text ends here, but in complete works, it continues with the recommended surahs for the second and third rak'ahs.

Key Terms
الفاتحة (al-Fatiha): The first chapter of the Quran, recited in every rak'ah of prayer.
سورة (surah): A chapter of the Quran, or any portion after al-Fatiha. The ruling allows any surah, but following the Prophet's practice is preferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Any surah the worshiper wishes is allowed
C) Only Surah al-Ikhlas is allowed
D) No surah should be recited

Key Takeaway
In witr prayer, reciting a surah after al-Fatiha is sunnah, not wajib, and any surah is permissible, though following the Prophet's example is best.
