Qunut in Witr: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on qunut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on qunut in witr and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, using the respective hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Qunut in witr: Hanafi limits to last half of Ramadan; Shafi'i places it in Fajr. The key is whether the Prophet's Fajr qunut was a regular practice or only for calamities.

This lesson examines a well-known inter-madhhab dispute: where does the qunut belong? The Hanafi school holds that qunut in witr is limited to the last half of Ramadan, while the Shafi'i school considers qunut in Fajr a regular sunnah. We will explore the textual evidence each side uses and the Hanafi response that reconciles the conflicting hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa, qunut in witr is only performed in the last half of Ramadan. The school also holds that qunut is not part of Fajr prayer.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say qunut in witr throughout the year, but the presented view is Abu Hanifa's.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qunut is a regular sunnah in Fajr prayer, after bowing in the second rak'ah. He does not consider qunut in witr as sunnah.
The Shafi'i position is based on the practice they attribute to the Prophet.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on hadith narrated by Muslim and others that the Prophet performed qunut in Fajr after bowing. They interpret this as a regular practice.
The text mentions 'أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the Prophet's qunut in Fajr was only for a month during a calamity (qunut al-nawazil), and then he stopped. Other hadith about qunut in Fajr are similarly interpreted as emergency supplications, not a regular sunnah.
The Hanafi proof includes the hadith of teaching the qunut which indicates it is for the whole year in witr.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafis do not recite qunut in Fajr, while Shafi'is do. Hanafis only recite qunut in witr in the last half of Ramadan (or throughout the year per the disciples), while Shafi'is do not recite qunut in witr. This affects the follower: a Hanafi behind a Shafi'i imam in Fajr remains silent during the qunut.
The passage also mentions permissibility of following a Shafi'i imam, which is a related issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ قُنوت الوتر عنده في النصف الأخير من رمضان فقط، دون غيره)، خلافاً للشَّافِعِي (١) له في الفجر" [٢]. [١] قوله : فقط ؛ لما أخرجه أبو داود : «إنّ أبي بن كعب له كـان يـؤمهم في التراويح، ويقنت في النصف الآخر من رمضان فقط ) ، ولنا : حديث تعليم النبي القنوت المروي في سنن أبي داود وغيره من الأحاديث الدالة على كونه في جميع السنة (٣) [٢] قوله : في الفجر ؛ فعنده السُنَّة أن يقنت في صلاة الفجر بعد ركوع الثانية ؛ لما روي أن النبي كان يفعل ذلك (4) ، أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم. ولنا ما روي أنه قنت شهراً يدعو على قبائل من الكفار، ثم ترك) ، أخرجه مسلم وغيره، والأحاديث الواردة في قنوته في الفجر وغيره كلها محمولة على قنوت النوازل ونحو ذلك ، كما حققه ابن القيم في زاد المعاد في هدي خير العباد». ويتبع القانت بعد ركوع الوتر لا القانت في الفجر ، بل يسكت [١]قوله : لا القانت ؛ أي لا يتبع المؤتم الإمام الذي يقرأ القنوت في الفجر، وهذه المسألة دلّت على جواز الاقتداء بالشافعية ، وفيه اختلاف كثير بين أصحابنا، والحق الصراح هو الجواز مطلقاً، كما حققه مؤلّف «الإتمام بمقلد كل إمام».
For qunut of witr according to him (Abu Hanifa) is only in the last half of Ramadan, not otherwise, contrary to al-Shafi'i who has it in Fajr. [1] His statement: 'only'; because of what Abu Dawud narrated that Ubayy ibn Ka'b used to lead them in tarawih and perform qunut in the last half of Ramadan only. And for us is the hadith of the Prophet teaching the qunut, narrated in Sunan Abu Dawud and other ahadith indicating it is throughout the whole year. [2] His statement: 'in Fajr'; for according to him (al-Shafi'i) the sunnah is to perform qunut in Fajr after the bowing of the second rak'ah, because of the narration that the Prophet used to do that, narrated by Muslim, the authors of the Sunan, and others. And for us is what is narrated that he performed qunut for a month supplicating against tribes of disbelievers, then left it, narrated by Muslim and others. All ahadith about his qunut in Fajr and other prayers are interpreted as qunut of calamities and the like, as verified by Ibn Qayyim in Zad al-Ma'ad. And the follower (muqtadi) follows the qunut after the bowing of witr, not the qunut in Fajr, but remains silent. [1] His statement: 'not the qanut'; i.e., the follower does not follow the imam who recites the qunut in Fajr, and this issue indicates the permissibility of following Shafi'is, and there is much disagreement among our scholars, but the clear truth is permissibility absolutely, as verified by the author of al-Itmam bi Muqalladi Kulli Imam.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the qunut prayer. It presents the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) that qunut in Witr is only performed in the last half of Ramadan, and then contrasts it with the Shafi'i position that qunut is sunnah in Fajr prayer. The author provides the proofs: for the Hanafi view, a hadith from Abu Dawud that Ubayy ibn Ka'b led tarawih qunut only in the last half of Ramadan, and also the hadith of the Prophet teaching the qunut supplication, which indicates it is recited throughout the year. For the Shafi'i view, they cite a hadith that the Prophet performed qunut in Fajr after bowing. The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's qunut in Fajr was only for a month during a calamity, and all such ahadith are interpreted as qunut al-nawazil (qunut during calamities), not a regular sunnah. The passage then adds a practical ruling: a Hanafi follower (muqtadi) should follow the imam in qunut of Witr, but if the imam performs qunut in Fajr (as Shafi'is do), the follower should remain silent and not recite the qunut. The footnote clarifies that this implies it is permissible for a Hanafi to pray behind a Shafi'i imam, which is a debated issue but the correct view is absolute permissibility.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited in prayer, specifically after bowing in the witr prayer (or in fajr according to Shafi'i).
النوازل (al-nawazil): Calamities or emergencies that befall the Muslim community; qunut al-nawazil is a special supplication during times of distress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) In every witr prayer throughout the year.
B) Only in the last half of Ramadan.
C) Only in Fajr prayer.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school places qunut in witr (not Fajr), and its performance is limited to the last half of Ramadan according to Abu Hanifa, while the Shafi'i school places it in Fajr, with each side relying on different hadith and interpretations.

Recitation in Witr Prayer: Ruling and Reasoning
Ruling with proof: recitation in witr and its legal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in each rak'ah of witr, identify the preferred surahs from prophetic practice, and explain the legal reasoning that distinguishes witr from Maghrib in requiring recitation in all three rak'ahs.

Core Idea
Witr requires recitation of Fatiha and a surah in every rak'ah because its obligation status is disputed, giving it the ruling of nafl prayer.

This passage from the commentaries of al-Hidayah addresses a key question in witr prayer: is recitation required in all three rak'ahs? The text first presents the basic ruling and preferred surahs from the Prophet's practice, then defends the Hanafi position against an objection that would limit recitation to two rak'ahs. Understanding this reasoning reveals an important principle: the legal nature of witr (between wajib and sunnah) shapes its ritual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling on recitation
Read the opening statement: 'He recites in every rak'ah of it the Fatiha and a surah.' This is the established ruling. Note that the commentator adds 'any surah he wishes' but recommends following the prophetic examples.
The ruling is clear: recitation of both Fatiha and an additional surah is required in each rak'ah.
2
Examine the prophetic examples of surahs
The passage lists multiple hadiths describing the surahs the Prophet recited in witr. Note the variants: combinations of Sabbih isma rabbika, Qul ya ayyuha al-kafirun, Qul huwa Allahu ahad, al-Mu'awwidhatayn, etc. These are the preferred (afdal) recitations, not obligatory.
The hadiths are ahad (single-transmitter) but collectively establish the practice.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues: In other prayers, recitation is only required in the first two rak'ahs (e.g., Maghrib has recitation only in first two, and in the last two of four-rak'ah prayers, silence suffices). By analogy, witr's third rak'ah should not require recitation. Why is witr different?
This objection relies on qiyas to Maghrib and other fard prayers.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: The disagreement among Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa: wajib; disciples: sunnah muakkadah) gives witr a 'semblance of nafl' (shubhat al-nafliyya). In supererogatory prayers, recitation is required in every rak'ah. Therefore, witr follows the nafl rule, not the fard rule.
The key point: the ikhtilaf within the school (or even cross-madhhab) affects the legal characterisation of witr and thus its rulings.
5
Apply the principle
This means that the mufta bihi position (that witr is sunnah muakkadah, not wajib) is consistent with requiring recitation in all three rak'ahs. If witr were considered wajib like fajr, it might follow the fard pattern. But the reliance on the sunnah-muakkadah view means it is treated like a supererogatory prayer in this regard.
The ruling on recitation is thus tied to the broader ikhtilaf on witr's obligation status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصلاة صلاة الوتر والنوافل دون غيره، ويقرأُ في كُلِّ ركعة منه الفاتحة، وسورة [٣] [٣] قوله : وسورة ؛ أي أي سورة شاء، والأولى الاتباع بما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وكان يقرأ تارة في الركعة الأولى من الوتر بـ سبح اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى ، وفي الثانية : ( قُلْ يَتَأَيُّهَا الكَفِرُونَ ﴾ ، وفي الثالثة : قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدُ وقُلْ : ﴿ أَعُوذُ بِرَبِّ الْفَلَقِ ( وَقُلْ أَعُوذُ بِرَبِّ النَّاسِ ) ، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجة وغيرهم. وورد أيضاً أنه كان يقرأ في الأولى : سبع أسم ، وفي الثانية : (قُلْ يَتَأَيُّهَا الكفرون ، وفي الثالثة : قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ ) ، ذكره الترمذي. وورد أيضاً أنه كان يقرأ في الأولى : الهَنكُمُ التَّكَاثُرُ ، وإِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ ) ، وإِذَا زُلْزِلَتِ ) ، وفي الثانية : ووَالْعَصْرِ ، وإِذَا جَاءَ نَصْرُ الله ، وإِنَّا أَعْطَيْنَاكَ الْكَوْنَرَ ، وفي الثالثة : قُلْ يَتَأَيُّهَا الكَفِرُونَ ) وتَبَّتْ ) ، وقُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ ) ، أخرجه أحمد وغيره. وقد بسط طرق هذه الأخبار الحافظ ابن حجر العسقلاني في تخريج أحاديث الأذكار المسمى بـ«نتائج الأفكار»، وبهذه الأخبار استدلّ أصحابنا على أنّ الوتر ثلاث ركعات. واعترض هاهنا بأنّ القراءة في الأوليين قراءة في الأُخريين في الرباعي، وفي الثالثة في الثلاثي عندنا ، حتى لو سكت في الأخيرين أجزأه عندنا، كما ثبت في موضعه، فينبغي أن تنوب القراءة في الأوليين عنها في ثالثة الوتر أيضاً، ولا يفترض فيها كما في صلاة المغرب. وأجيب عنه : بأن الاختلاف في كون الوتر واجباً أو سنة أورث شبهة النفلية، وفي صلاة التطوّع سنّة كان أو غيرها تفترض القراءة في جميع الركعات فكذا هذا. كذا في شروح «الهداية».
The prayer: the prayer of witr and supererogatory prayers, not other than that. And he recites in every rak'ah of it the Fatiha and a surah. [3] His statement: 'and a surah'; i.e., any surah he wishes, but the better is following what is established from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. And he would sometimes recite in the first rak'ah of witr 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High', and in the second 'Say: O disbelievers', and in the third 'Say: He is Allah, the One' and 'Say: I seek refuge with the Lord of the daybreak' and 'Say: I seek refuge with the Lord of mankind', narrated by Abu Dawud, at-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and others. And it is also transmitted that he would recite in the first 'Glorify the name of your Lord', in the second 'Say: O disbelievers', and in the third 'Say: He is Allah, the One', mentioned by at-Tirmidhi. And it is also transmitted that he would recite in the first 'The mutual rivalry for piling up...' and 'Indeed, We sent it down in the Night of Decree' and 'When the earth is shaken...', and in the second 'By the time' and 'When the victory of Allah comes' and 'Indeed, We have granted you al-Kawthar', and in the third 'Say: O disbelievers' and 'Perish' and 'Say: He is Allah, the One', narrated by Ahmad and others. And al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani has elaborated the chains of these reports in his Takhrij Ahadith al-Adhkar named Nata'ij al-Afkar. And with these reports our companions have argued that witr is three rak'ahs. And an objection is raised here: that the recitation in the first two (rak'ahs) is recitation in the last two in the four-rak'ah prayer, and in the third in the three-rak'ah prayer according to us, such that if one remains silent in the last two it suffices him according to us, as established in its place, so it should be that the recitation in the first two suffices for the third of witr as well, and it is not obligatory (muftarad) in it as in Maghrib prayer. And it is answered: that the difference of opinion about witr being obligatory (wajib) or sunnah has given it the semblance of supererogatory (nafl) prayer, and in supererogatory prayer, whether sunnah or other, recitation is obligatory (taftarid) in all rak'ahs, so likewise this. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the recitation in witr prayer. It first states the basic ruling: in each rak'ah of witr, one recites Surah al-Fatihah and another surah. The commentator then gives the preferred recitation based on established sunnah: the Prophet used to recite various combinations, such as in the first rak'ah 'Sabbih isma rabbika al-a'la', in the second 'Qul ya ayyuha al-kafirun', and in the third 'Qul huwa Allahu ahad' plus the mu'awwidhatayn (al-Falaq and an-Nas). Other combinations are also mentioned. These hadiths are used by Hanafi scholars to prove that witr is three rak'ahs. The passage then addresses an objection: since in other prayers (like Maghrib or the last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer) recitation is not obligatory (only in the first two), why is it required in all three rak'ahs of witr? The answer is that witr has a semblance of nafl (supererogatory) prayer because of the disagreement on its obligation level. In nafl prayer, recitation is required in every rak'ah. Therefore, witr follows the same rule. This reasoning is from the commentaries of al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الوتر (al-witr): A prayer performed after the night prayer, consisting of an odd number of rak'ahs (usually 3), considered by Abu Hanifa as wajib and by others as sunnah.
تفترض القراءة (taftarid al-qira'ah): Recitation is obligatory (i.e., required) in every rak'ah of nafl prayer, unlike fard prayer where it is only required in the first two rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
Recitation of Fatiha and a surah is wajib in every rak'ah of witr due to its supererogatory nature arising from the debate on its obligation status.

Following the Imam in Qunut of Witr and Fajr: Abrogation vs Ijtihad
Ruling on follower's participation in qunut of witr vs Fajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a follower's participation in qunut during witr and Fajr prayers, and apply the distinction between abrogated and ijtihad-based practices.

Core Idea
Follower joins imam in witr qunut (ijtihad) but remains silent in Fajr qunut (proven abrogated).

When a Hanafi follower prays behind a Shafi'i imam, he may encounter qunut recited in different positions. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the qunut of witr, where timing is a matter of ijtihad, and the qunut of Fajr, which is considered abrogated. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proofs, and the internal Hanafi debate between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two cases: (1) imam does qunut in witr after ruku', (2) imam does qunut in Fajr. For each, determine the follower's action.
The ruling differs based on whether the practice is considered abrogated or a matter of ijtihad.
2
Analyze the dalil for witr qunut position
Examine the hadith evidence cited: Hanafi reliance on Anas (before ruku') and Shafi'i reliance on Suwaid (after ruku'). Understand how each school uses the evidence.
Hanafi: qunut before ruku' is the norm; the after ruku' practice was only for one month.
3
Examine the Fajr qunut dispute
Note that the Hanafi school considers Fajr qunut abrogated. Sahibayn say follower remains silent; Abu Yusuf argues he should follow, analogizing to Eid takbirs.
The key difference: known abrogation vs. undetermined ijtihad.
4
Apply the principle: no following in abrogated acts
Understand the analogy to funeral prayer: if imam says five takbirs, follower does not follow because the fifth is abrogated. Similarly, Fajr qunut is abrogated, so no following.
This principle is used by Sahibayn to rebut Abu Yusuf's analogy.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Sahibayn: follower remains silent during Fajr qunut. The text indicates this by stating 'this is according to them (Sahibayn)' and their response.
In case of a Hanafi follower behind a Shafi'i imam doing Fajr qunut, the follower should not participate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتبع القانت بعد ركوع الوتر لا القانت في الفجر، بل يسكت (قوله : لا القانت؛ أي لا يتبع المؤتم الإمام الذي يقرأ القنوت في الفجر، وهذه المسألة دلّت على جواز الاقتداء بالشافعية، وفيه اختلاف كثير بين أصحابنا، والحق الصراح هو الجواز مطلقاً، كما حققه مؤلّف «الإتمام بمقلد كل إمام». أي إن قرأ الإمام قنوت الوتر بعد الرُكُوع يتبعه المقتدي، وإن قنت الإمام في الفجر لا يتبعه المقتدي، بل يسكت (١) قوله : الإمام قنوت الوتر بعد الركوع؛ كما إذا كان الإمام شافعياً، فإن قنوت الوتر عندهم بعد الركوع لما أخرجه الدارقطني عن سويد قال : «سمعت أبا بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلياً يقولون : قنت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في آخر الوتر، وكانوا يفعلون ذلك». واستدل أصحابنا بما أخرجه مسلم وغيره عن عاصم الأحول : «سألت أنساً رضي الله عنه عن القنوت في الصلاة، قال: نعم، فقلت : أكان قبل الركوع أو بعد الركوع ؟ قال : قبله، قلت : فإن فلاناً أخبرني عنك أنك قلت : بعده ، قال : كذب إنما قنت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد الركوع شهراً). وأخرج ابن أبي شَيْبَة : «إنّ ابن مسعود لله وأصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كانوا يقنتون في الوتر قبل الركوع )، ولو نسي الإمام الحنفي القنوت قبل الركوع ثم تذكره في الركوع لا يقنت؛ لفوات محله، ويسجد للسهو بترك الواجب، ولا يعود إلى القيام، فإن عاد إليه وقنت لم تفسد صلاته. كذا في «الدر المختار». (٢) قوله : يتبعه المقتدي؛ أي يقنت هو أيضاً بعد الركوع تبعاً لإمامه؛ لأن كونه بعد الركوع أو قبله مجتهد فيه، وليس كونه بعد الركوع مقطوعا بنسخه أو بعدم سنيته فلا يخالف إمامه في مثل هذا بخلاف القنوت في الفجر عندنا، فإنه ثبت نسخه، وأنه فعله ثم تركه، ولا متابعة في المنسوخ كما إذا كبر إمامُ الجنازة خمساً لا يتابعه المؤتم في التكبيرة الخامسة؛ لثبوت نسخها. [٣] قوله : بل يسكت؛ هذا عندهما، وقال أبو يوسف له : يتبعه كما في تكبيرات العيدين إذا زاد إمامه على الثلاث؛ لأنه تبع لإمامه في فصل مجتهد فيه، وجوابهما أنه لا متابعة في ما علم نسخه.
And he follows the one who does qunut after the bowing of witr, not the one who does qunut in Fajr; rather he keeps silent. (His statement: 'not the one who does qunut' i.e. the follower does not follow the imam who recites qunut in Fajr. This issue indicates the permissibility of following a Shafi'i, and there is much disagreement among our companions, and the clear truth is absolute permissibility, as verified by the author of 'al-Itmam bi-Muqallad Kulli Imam'. That is: if the imam recites the qunut of witr after the bowing, the follower follows him; and if the imam does qunut in Fajr, the follower does not follow him, but remains silent. (1) His statement: 'the imam does qunut of witr after bowing' as when the imam is Shafi'i, for their qunut of witr is after bowing according to what al-Daraqutni narrated from Suwaid who said: 'I heard Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali say: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ did qunut at the end of witr, and they used to do that.' And our companions adduced what Muslim and others narrated from 'Asim al-Ahwal: 'I asked Anas رضي الله عنه about qunut in prayer, he said: Yes. I said: Was it before bowing or after bowing? He said: Before it. I said: So-and-so informed me from you that you said: After it. He said: He lied; the Messenger of Allah ﷺ only did qunut after bowing for a month.' And Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated that 'Ibn Mas'ud and the companions of the Prophet ﷺ used to do qunut in witr before bowing. And if the Hanafi imam forgot the qunut before bowing and then remembered during bowing, he does not do qunut because its place is missed, and he performs prostrations of forgetfulness for leaving an obligatory act, and he does not return to standing; if he returns and does qunut, his prayer is not invalidated. So in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar'. (2) His statement: 'the follower follows him' i.e. he also does qunut after bowing following his imam, because whether it is after bowing or before is a matter of ijtihad, and its being after bowing is not conclusively abrogated or lacking in sunnah, so he does not oppose his imam in such a matter, unlike qunut in Fajr in our view, for its abrogation is established, and he did it then left it, and there is no following in the abrogated, just as if the imam of the funeral prayer says five takbirs, the follower does not follow him in the fifth takbir due to its abrogation being established. (3) His statement: 'rather he keeps silent'—this is according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: He should follow him, just as in the takbirs of the two Eids if the imam adds beyond three, because he follows his imam in a matter of ijtihad. Their response: There is no following in what is known to be abrogated.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the ruling regarding a follower's (muqtadi) participation in qunut when praying behind an imam. It is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya. The core ruling: The follower should join the imam in qunut of witr when it is performed after ruku', but when the imam recites qunut in Fajr prayer, the follower should remain silent and not join. The commentary first clarifies that this ruling implies permissibility of praying behind a Shafi'i imam who does qunut in witr after ruku'. It then provides hadith evidence: the Shafi'i rely on a narration from Suwaid that the four caliphs reported the Prophet did qunut at the end of witr (after ruku'); the Hanafis rely on a narration from Anas that the Prophet did qunut before ruku' except for one month, and on the practice of Ibn Mas'ud praying qunut before ruku'. The Hanafi position is that qunut in witr is before ruku', but if one forgets and remembers during ruku', he does not go back; he performs sajda sahw. The commentary then addresses a dispute between Abu Yusuf and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to the two (Sahibayn), if the imam does qunut in Fajr, the follower remains silent. Abu Yusuf argues that the follower should follow, analogizing to Eid takbirs where if the imam adds beyond three, the follower follows because it is a matter of ijtihad. The two respond that there is no following in what is known to be abrogated (like Fajr qunut). The relied-upon position is that of the two.

Key Terms
قنوت (qunut): A supplication recited in a specific posture in prayer; in witr it is wajib before ruku' in Hanafi school.
مقتدي (muqtadi): A person praying behind an imam (follower).
وتر (witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after Isha, consisting of three rak'ahs in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Hanafi follower prays behind a Shafi'i imam who recites qunut in Fajr prayer, what should the follower do according to the relied-upon position?
A) He should recite the qunut with the imam.
B) He should remain silent and not recite qunut.
C) He should leave the prayer.
D) He should recite a different supplication.

Key Takeaway
The follower joins the imam in witr qunut (ijtihad) but remains silent in Fajr qunut (abrogated).

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Following the Imam in Qunut: Witr vs Fajr
Inter-madhhab dispute on qunut and mutaba'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a Hanafi follower must follow the imam in qunut (witr) and when they must remain silent (Fajr), applying the principle of mutaba'ah and the concept of abrogation.

Core Idea
Follow in matters of ijtihad, not in matters known to be abrogated.

The principle of mutaba'ah requires the follower to follow the imam in most actions of prayer. However, when an action is known to be abrogated (mansukh), following would be incorrect. This lesson examines the case of qunut: the Hanafi school holds that qunut in witr is a matter of ijtihad, so the follower follows even if the imam performs it after bowing. But qunut in Fajr is abrogated, so the follower must not follow; instead he remains silent standing. We also explore an intra-Hanafi difference where Abu Yusuf argued for following in all cases of ijtihad, including Fajr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two situations: (1) Imam recites qunut of witr after bowing (as Shafi'i imam would), and (2) Imam recites qunut in Fajr prayer. Note the Hanafi position on each.
Witr qunut is wajib before bowing in Hanafi; Fajr qunut is abrogated.
2
Apply the principle of mutaba'ah
Understand that mutaba'ah is wajib in actions that are part of the prayer and not abrogated. If the imam's action is based on ijtihad (like timing of witr qunut), the follower follows. If it is known to be abrogated, the follower does not follow.
Abrogation (naskh) is determined by clear evidence that the Prophet ﷺ performed then left the practice.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text mentions that Abu Yusuf disagreed with the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) regarding Fajr qunut. He argued that the follower should follow because it is a matter of ijtihad, like the extra takbirs in Eid prayer. Examine why the majority disagreed: because Fajr qunut is known to be abrogated, whereas the timing of witr qunut is not.
The comparison with funeral prayer (five takbirs) is used: since the fifth takbir is abrogated, the follower does not follow.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The commentary states that the correct view (al-asahh) is that the follower remains silent standing (yaskutu qa'iman) when the imam recites Fajr qunut. This means the follower does not follow in the qunut but also does not sit down in protest; rather he stands silently with hands at his sides.
Silent standing avoids both imitating an abrogated act and being inattentive (since standing is part of prayer posture).

Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding qunut in witr and Fajr prayers. The Hanafi position is that qunut in witr is before bowing, and the follower must follow the imam in this if he performs it after bowing, because it is a matter of ijtihad. However, if the imam recites qunut in Fajr, the Hanafi follower should not follow because it is abrogated. The text provides evidence from hadith for both positions. It also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Yusuf disagrees with the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, saying the follower should follow the imam even in Fajr qunut because it is a matter of ijtihad. The commentary clarifies that the correct position is that the follower remains silent standing, not sitting or prostrating, to avoid following in an abrogated act while still maintaining the posture of prayer. The passage also provides a practical ruling for a Hanafi imam who forgets qunut: he does not return to it, but performs sajdah sahw.

Key Terms
قنوت الوتر (qunut al-witr): The supplication recited in the witr prayer; in Hanafi fiqh it is performed before bowing.
متابعة (mutaaba'ah): Following the imam in actions of prayer.
منسوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; a ruling that has been repealed by a later revelation or sunnah.
قنوت الفجر (qunut al-fajr): The qunut recited in Fajr prayer. In Hanafi, it is abrogated; in Shafi'i, it is sunnah after ruku.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Recite qunut before bowing.
B) Remain silent and not recite qunut.
C) Recite qunut after bowing following the imam.
D) Leave the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The follower follows the imam in matters of ijtihad (like witr qunut timing) but does not follow in actions known to be abrogated (like Fajr qunut).

Sunan Rawatib: Confirmed Sunnah Prayers
Lists the confirmed sunnah prayers before and after fard prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of rak'ahs for each confirmed sunnah prayer and identify them as sunnah mu'akkadah.

Core Idea
Twelve rak'ahs of sunnah rawatib are confirmed; leaving them is makruh tanzihi.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain sunnah prayers are emphasized as 'mu'akkadah' – the Prophet ﷺ consistently performed them. This lesson outlines their exact numbers and times, based on authentic hadith. Memorizing this list is essential for understanding the structure of a Muslim's daily voluntary prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah prayers before fard
List the sunnah prayers that come before the obligatory prayer (Fajr, Zuhr, Jumu'ah).
2
Identify the sunnah prayers after fard
List the sunnah prayers after Zuhr, Maghrib, Isha.
3
Note the number of rak'ahs
For each, state the specific number: 2 before Fajr, 4 before Zuhr, 2 after Zuhr, 2 after Maghrib, 2 after Isha; for Jumu'ah, 4 before and 4 after.
4
Understand the ruling level
These are sunnah mu'akkadah; leaving them is makruh tanzihi, not sinful but disliked.
5
Memorize the hadith evidence
The hadith from Tirmidhi listing 12 rak'ahs, and the practice of Aisha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسن قبل الفجر، وبعد الظهر، والمغرب والعشاء ركعتان، وقبل الظهر، والجمعة وبعدها أربع بتسليمة واحدة
(۳) قوله : وسُن؛ بضم السين، وتشديد النون: شروع في بيان السنن المؤكدة الرواتب المتقدمة على الفرائض أو المتأخرة عنها بعد الفراغ من ذكر الواجب، وبعد الفراغ منه شرع بقوله : وجب في المستحبّات رعاية؛ لتقديم الأهم فالأهم. [٤] قوله : ركعتان؛ لحديث: «من صلى في يوم وليلة ثنتي عشرة ركعة سوى المكتوبة بني له بيت في الجنة أربعاً قبل الظهر، وركعتين بعدها، وركعتين بعد المغرب، وركعتين بعد العشاء، وركعتين قبل الفجر أخرجه الترمذي وغيره.
(۳)
، وعن عائشة رضي ا الله عنها : «كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يُصلّي في بيتي قبل الظهر أربعاً، ثم يخرج فيصلي الظهر، ثم يدخل فيصلي ركعتين ، ثم يخرج فيصلي بالناس العصر ، ويصلي المغرب ثم يدخل فيصلي ركعتين ، ثم يصلي بالناس العشاء، ويدخل بيتي فيصلي ركعتين، وكان إذا طلع الفجر صلى ركعتين ) ، أخرجه مسلم وأبو داود واحمد وغيرهم.

And it is sunnah: before Fajr, after Zuhr, Maghrib, and Isha two rak'ahs each; and before Zuhr and Jumu'ah and after them four rak'ahs with one taslim. (3) His saying: 'wa sunna' with dammah on the sin and shaddah on the noon: beginning of the explanation of the confirmed sunnah prayers (rawatib) that precede or follow the obligatory prayers after finishing mentioning the obligatory acts. [4] His saying: 'two rak'ahs' due to the hadith: 'Whoever prays twelve rak'ahs in a day and night besides the prescribed prayers, a house will be built for him in Paradise: four before Zuhr, two after it, two after Maghrib, two after Isha, and two before Fajr' narrated by Tirmidhi and others. Also from Aisha: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) used to pray in my house four before Zuhr, then go out and pray Zuhr, then come back and pray two, then go out and pray Asr with the people, then pray Maghrib, then come back and pray two, then pray Isha with the people, then come to my house and pray two, and when Fajr came he prayed two.' Narrated by Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ahmad, and others. (4) [0] His saying: 'four with one taslim' meaning one salutation at the end, as for the recommendation of four before Zuhr...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing the confirmed sunnah prayers (sunan rawatib mu'akkadah). The matn states: It is sunnah to pray two rak'ahs before Fajr, after Zuhr, after Maghrib, and after Isha; and four rak'ahs before Zuhr and before and after Jumu'ah, all with one taslim. The commentary explains that 'sunnah' here refers to mu'akkadah (confirmed). It provides two hadiths as evidence: one from Tirmidhi mentioning 12 rak'ahs, and one from Aisha describing the Prophet's practice. The commentary also notes that the four rak'ahs before Zuhr are recommended with one taslim (i.e., prayed as two sets of two? Actually the text says four with one taslim meaning continuous four rak'ahs with one salutation at the end, as per Hanafi fiqh). The passage emphasizes the virtue of these prayers and their fixed times.

Key Terms
سنن رواتب (sunan rawatib): Confirmed sunnah prayers that are regularly performed before or after the obligatory prayers.
أربع بتسليمة واحدة (arba' bi-taslimah wahidah): Four rak'ahs performed consecutively with one final salutation at the end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Four rak'ahs.
B) Two rak'ahs.
C) Three rak'ahs.
D) Six rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
The confirmed sunnah prayers (sunan rawatib) consist of 12 rak'ahs: 2 before Fajr, 4 before and 2 after Zuhr, 2 after Maghrib, 2 after Isha, and for Jumu'ah, 4 before and 4 after.

Salah — Prayer

Sunnan Rawatib: Number of Rak'at and Their Proofs
Ruling with proof: enumeration of rawatib rak'at and hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of rak'at for each sunnah rawatib prayer and identify the primary hadith proofs that establish them as sunnah mu'akkadah in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Prophet's consistent practice establishes the rawatib as twelve rak'at of confirmed sunnah.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on the regular sunnah prayers (rawatib) that accompany the five daily prayers. The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lists the specific number of rak'at for each prayer and provides several authentic hadith to support the ruling. Understanding these rawatib helps you complete the sunnah of the Prophet and earn the reward promised in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, determine what act is being ruled upon (the rawatib prayers) and the level of the ruling (sunnah mu'akkadah). Notice that the author uses the verb 'sunn' to indicate a confirmed sunnah.
The Hanafi school distinguishes sunnah mu'akkadah from mustahabb. The rawatib are sunnah mu'akkadah, meaning the Prophet consistently performed them and omission is disliked.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites multiple hadith. Identify the primary hadith that enumerates the twelve rak'at: 'Whoever prays twelve rak'at in a day and night...' Also note the hadith from Aisha describing the Prophet's practice.
The hadith is reported by Tirmidhi, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and others.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling. The explicit enumeration indicates that these specific prayers are sunnah. The promise of a house in Paradise indicates strong recommendation (sunnah mu'akkadah), not obligation.
The imperative 'prays' in the context of reward implies desirability, not obligation.
4
Note the specifics: four rak'at with one taslim
For the sets of four rak'at (before Zuhr, before and after Jumu'ah), the author specifies they are performed with one taslimah (single salutation). This is supported by specific hadith about four before Zuhr without separating.
This distinguishes them from the two-rak'at sets which are performed with a taslim after each pair.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage mentions that Abu Yusuf held the sunnah after Jumu'ah to be six rak'at, based on the practice of Ibn Umar. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is four rak'at, as stated in the matn.
This ikhtilaf shows the range of valid practice within the Hanafi school.

Source Text

وسن قبل الفجر، وبعد الظهر، والمغرب والعشاء ركعتان، وقبل الظهر، والجمعة وبعدها أربع بتسليمة واحدة (وسن قبل الفجر، وبعد الظهر، والمغرب والعشاء ركعتان، وقبل الظهر، والجمعة وبعدها أربع بتسليمة واحدة) (وسن) بضم السين، وتشديد النون: شروع في بيان السنن المؤكدة الرواتب المتقدمة على الفرائض أو المتأخرة عنها بعد الفراغ من ذكر الواجب، وبعد الفراغ منه شرع بقوله: وجب في المستحبّات رعاية؛ لتقديم الأهم فالأهم. (ركعتان) لحديث: «من صلى في يوم وليلة ثنتي عشرة ركعة سوى المكتوبة بني له بيت في الجنة أربعاً قبل الظهر، وركعتين بعدها، وركعتين بعد المغرب، وركعتين بعد العشاء، وركعتين قبل الفجر» أخرجه الترمذي وغيره. وعن عائشة رضي الله عنها: «كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يصلّي في بيتي قبل الظهر أربعاً، ثم يخرج فيصلي الظهر، ثم يدخل فيصلي ركعتين، ثم يخرج فيصلي بالناس العصر، ويصلي المغرب ثم يدخل فيصلي ركعتين، ثم يصلي بالناس العشاء، ويدخل بيتي فيصلي ركعتين، وكان إذا طلع الفجر صلى ركعتين» أخرجه مسلم وأبو داود وأحمد وغيرهم. (أربع بتسليمة) أي بسلام واحد في الآخر، أما استنان الأربع قبل الظهر فلما مر ذكره، والحديث: «أربع قبل الظهر ليس فيهن تسليم يفتح لهن أبواب السماء» أخرجه أبو داود وغيره، وفي «سنن ابن ماجة»: «إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يصلي قبل الظهر أربعاً لا يفصل بينهن بتسليم». وأما استنانُ الأربع قبل الجمعة وبعدها فلحديث: «إذا صلّى أحدكم الجمعة فليصل بعدها أربع ركعات» أخرجه مسلم. وعن ابن مسعود: «كان النبي الله يصلي قبل الجمعة أربعاً وبعدها أربعاً» قاله الترمذي. وروى أبو داود والترمذي عن ابن عمر: أنه كان يصلي بعد الجمعة ستاً تارة ركعتين، ثم أربعاً، وتارة أربعاً ثم ركعتين، وفي الصحيح عن ابن عمر: «أنه كان إذا كان بمكة فصلّى الجمعة تقدَّم فصلى ركعتين، ثم تقدم فصلّى أربعاً، وإذا كان بالمدينة فصلى الجمعة رجع إلى بيته، فصلى ركعتين، ولم يصل في المسجد، فقيل له في ذلك، فقال: كان رسول الله يفعل ذلك»، وبهذا ذهب أبو يوسف إلى أن المسنون بعد الجمعة ست ركعات. [قوله: وحبب؛ بصيغة المجهول من باب التفعيل؛ أي استحب وذلك لحديث: «رحم الله امرأ صلّى قبل العصر أربعاً» أخرجه الترمذي وحسنه.
And it is sunnah: before Fajr, after Zuhr, Maghrib, and Isha: two rak'at; and before Zuhr, Jumu'ah, and after them: four rak'at with one taslimah. (It is sunnah) with dammah on the sin and tashdid on the nun: beginning the explanation of the confirmed regular sunnahs that precede or follow the obligatory prayers, after finishing mentioning the obligatory, and after finishing it he began with his statement: 'and it is obligatory in recommended acts to observe' to give precedence to the most important. (Two rak'at) due to the hadith: 'Whoever prays in a day and night twelve rak'at besides the obligatory, a house will be built for him in Paradise: four before Zuhr, two after it, two after Maghrib, two after Isha, and two before Fajr' reported by at-Tirmidhi and others. And from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to pray in my house four before Zuhr, then go out and pray Zuhr, then enter and pray two rak'at, then go out and pray Asr with the people, then pray Maghrib, then enter and pray two rak'at, then pray Isha with the people, and enter my house and pray two rak'at, and when dawn came he prayed two rak'at' reported by Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ahmad, and others. (Four with one taslimah) i.e., with one salutation at the end. As for the recommendation of four before Zuhr, it is due to what was mentioned, and the hadith: 'Four before Zuhr with no taslim between them, the doors of heaven are opened for them' reported by Abu Dawud and others, and in Sunan Ibn Majah: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to pray four before Zuhr without separating them with taslim.' As for the recommendation of four before Jumu'ah and after it, it is due to the hadith: 'When one of you prays Jumu'ah, let him pray four rak'at after it' reported by Muslim. And from Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to pray four before Jumu'ah and four after it' said by at-Tirmidhi. And Abu Dawud and at-Tirmidhi narrated from Ibn Umar that he used to pray six after Jumu'ah, sometimes two then four, sometimes four then two. And in Sahih from Ibn Umar: 'When he was in Makkah and prayed Jumu'ah, he would go forward and pray two rak'at, then go forward and pray four; and when he was in Madinah and prayed Jumu'ah, he would return to his house and pray two rak'at, and would not pray in the mosque. He was asked about that, and he said: The Messenger of Allah used to do that.' And due to this, Abu Yusuf held that the sunnah after Jumu'ah is six rak'at. [His statement: 'and it is beloved' in the passive form from the pattern of taf'il, i.e., recommended, due to the hadith: 'May Allah have mercy on a man who prays four before Asr' reported by at-Tirmidhi and he deemed it good.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm wa dalil) from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It explains the confirmed regular sunnah prayers (sunan rawatib mu'akkadah) that accompany the five daily prayers. The author first lists the number of rak'at: two before Fajr, two after Zuhr, two after Maghrib, two after Isha, and four before Zuhr, four before Jumu'ah, and four after Jumu'ah, each set of four performed with one taslim (single salutation at the end). The term 'sunnah' here means sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah), which is the Hanafi position. The proof includes multiple hadith: a hadith promising a house in Paradise for praying twelve rak'at of rawatib daily; the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as reported by Aisha; specific hadith for four before Zuhr without taslim; the command to pray four after Jumu'ah; and the practice of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Umar. The passage also notes a difference of opinion: Abu Yusuf considered six rak'at after Jumu'ah as sunnah, based on Ibn Umar's practice. Finally, it mentions that four rak'at before Asr are 'beloved' (mustahabb), not confirmed sunnah, supported by a hadith. Thus, the Hanafi school holds that the rawatib are twelve rak'at in total: two before Fajr, four before Zuhr, two after Zuhr, two after Maghrib, two after Isha (and four before and after Jumu'ah replace the Zuhr rawatib on Friday). The additional four before Asr are merely recommended, not regular sunnah.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): A confirmed recommended act (sunnah mu'akkadah) that the Prophet consistently performed; omission is disliked but not sinful.
راتبة (ratibah (pl. rawatib)): A regular voluntary prayer associated with an obligatory prayer, either before or after it.
تسليمة واحدة (taslimah wahidah): Performing a prayer with one salutation at the end, meaning no tashahhud or taslim between the four rak'at.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many rak'at of sunnah rawatib are performed before Zuhr?
A) Two rak'at
B) Four rak'at with one taslimah
C) Four rak'at with two taslimahs
D) Six rak'at

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds twelve rak'at of confirmed sunnah rawatib: two before Fajr, four before Zuhr, two after Zuhr, two after Maghrib, and two after Isha; on Friday, the Zuhr rawatib are replaced by four before and four after Jumu'ah.

The Sunan of Jumu'ah: Four or Six Rak'ahs After?
Ruling with proof on the sunan of Jumu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of rak'ahs before and after Jumu'ah, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and evaluate the evidence.

Core Idea
The Sunnah after Jumu'ah is four rak'ahs, but Abu Yusuf's view of six is also a valid Hanafi position.

In this passage, the author discusses the recommendation of four rak'ahs before and after Jumu'ah. While the majority of the Hanafi school holds firmly to four, a significant difference within the school — arising from the practice of Ibn 'Umar — leads Abu Yusuf to prefer six. Understanding the proof structure and the basis of the ikhtilaf is essential for giving a complete fatwa on this matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying additional rak'ahs before and after Jumu'ah; the ruling is mustahabb/sunnah, emphasized (sunnah mu'akkadah).
The author uses the term 'hubba' (beloved) to indicate recommendation.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith from Muslim: 'When one of you prays Jumu'ah, let him pray four rak'ahs after it.' This command indicates recommendation (nadb) not obligation.
The imperative 'falyusalli' is interpreted as nadb due to context and other evidence.
3
Note the variant practice from Ibn 'Umar
Ibn 'Umar's practice of six rak'ahs (two then four or four then two) provides the basis for Abu Yusuf's view.
The text reports that Ibn 'Umar varied his practice, sometimes doing two then four, sometimes four then two.
4
Evaluate the disagreement
Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold to four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah based on the explicit hadith and the practice of Ibn Mas'ud. Abu Yusuf prefers six, combining the two narrations.
The text presents Abu Yusuf's view as a valid alternative but the majority position remains four.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah, though six is also practiced and considered permissible.
The tarjih authority is the majority of the school and the explicit hadith.

Book of Prayer: Chapter on Witr and Supererogatory Prayers. [He said:] 'The four (rak'ahs) are beloved, and the four are beloved.' 217 When its mention passed, and the hadith: 'Four before Dhuhr without taslim between them, the doors of heaven are opened for them,' reported by Abu Dawud and others. And in Sunan Ibn Majah: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) used to pray four before Dhuhr without separating them with taslim.' And from Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) used to pray four before Jumu'ah and four after it,' narrated by al-Tirmidhi. Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Ibn 'Umar that he used to pray six after Jumu'ah: sometimes two then four, sometimes four then two. And in the Sahih from Ibn 'Umar: 'When he was in Makkah and prayed Jumu'ah, he would go forward and pray two rak'ahs, then go forward and pray four. When in Madinah, he would return home and pray two rak'ahs, not praying in the mosque. He was asked about this and said: The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) used to do that.' Based on this, Abu Yusuf held that the Sunnah after Jumu'ah is six rak'ahs. [1] His statement: 'And beloved' is in the passive voice from the form taf'il, meaning recommended, due to the hadith: 'May Allah have mercy on a person who prays four rak'ahs before 'Asr,' reported by al-Tirmidhi and classed as hasan. References to Sunan Abi Dawud, Sunan Ibn Majah, Sahih Muslim, Sunan al-Tirmidhi, and others.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the recommended (mustahabb) rak'ahs before and after the Friday prayer (Jumu'ah). The author first mentions that four rak'ahs before Dhuhr and before Jumu'ah are beloved, citing a hadith about four before Dhuhr with no taslim, and another hadith specifically for Jumu'ah from Muslim: pray four after Jumu'ah. Then he notes the practice of Ibn Mas'ud and the variation from Ibn 'Umar. From these narrations, Abu Yusuf derived that the Sunnah after Jumu'ah is six rak'ahs, combining the two, two, and four. The Hanafi position is that the preferred number is four rak'ahs after Jumu'ah, though the dispute remains. The commentary explains that the word 'hubba' (beloved) means mustahabb, and supports it with a hadith about praying four before 'Asr. Thus, the ruling is that the recommended is four rak'ahs before and after Jumu'ah, but Abu Yusuf's view of six is also within the school.

Key Terms
حبب (ḥubba): Passive verb meaning 'was made beloved', i.e., recommended (mustahabb) in the Hanafi terminology.
المسنون (al-masnūn): The established Sunnah (recommended practice) according to the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two rak'ahs
B) Four rak'ahs
C) Six rak'ahs
D) Eight rak'ahs

Key Takeaway
The Sunnah after Jumu'ah is four rak'ahs according to the majority Hanafi view, but the six rak'ah practice of Abu Yusuf is also a valid transmitted opinion within the school.

Dislike of Exceeding Four Rak'ahs Day and Eight at Night in Nafl
Ruling with proof on nafl rak'ah limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum rak'ahs per taslim in nafl prayers during the day and at night, and explain the basis for the dislike.

Core Idea
Voluntary prayers have limits: four rak'ahs per taslim by day, eight by night; exceeding is slightly disliked.

You know the immense reward of supererogatory prayers. But did you know that the Hanafi school sets specific limits on how many rak'ahs you can pray with one taslim? This lesson explores the ruling that it is disliked to exceed four rak'ahs during the day and eight at night, based on the absence of prophetic practice. We'll also examine a disagreement about the nighttime limit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that it is disliked (kuriḥa) to perform more than four rak'ahs with one taslim in daytime nafl, and more than eight in nighttime nafl. Note the Hanafi term 'kuriḥa' usually indicates makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked).
The ruling is for nafl prayers, not for sunnah prayers like tahajjud.
2
Locate the dalil
For the daytime limit, the author cites a hadith praising those who pray four before and after Dhuhr. The general practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) is also evidence.
The hadith is: 'Whoever guards four before Dhuhr and four after it, Allah will make him forbidden to the Fire' (al-Tirmidhi).
3
Understand the reasoning
The dislike is based on the fact that the Prophet (ﷺ) never exceeded these numbers, and if it were permissible, it would have been reported occasionally.
This is a qiyas of 'adam al-wujdan' (absence of evidence) indicating prohibition or dislike.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the night limit
The commentator questions the nighttime limit, citing reports that the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed nine rak'ahs (likely referring to eight nafl plus witr). This leads to a disagreement among Hanafi scholars.
The mufta bihi remains the dislike of exceeding eight, but some later scholars allow up to eight without dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبل العصر والعشاء وبعده وكره مزيد النفل على أربع بتسليمة نهاراً، وعلى ثمانٍ ليلا 218 عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية [1] قوله : قبل العصر ; وبعد الظهر أربعاً، حبب الأربع ؛ لحديث: «مَن حافظ على أربع قبل الظهر وأربع بعدها حرمه الله على النار ) ، أخرجه الترمذي وغيره. [٢] قوله : وكُره ; بصيغة المجهول ; أي يكره أداء النفل زائداً على أربع ركعات بسلام واحدٍ في النوافل النهارية، وعلى ثمان في النوافل الليلية، وعللوها بأنها لم ترو ولم يرد أنه زاد على الأربع بسلام واحد نهاراً ، وعلى ثمان ليلا، ولو لم تكره لروى ولو أحياناً تعليماً للجواز. ظاهر هذا التعليل أن الكراهة تنزيهية، ثمّ كلامهم في إنكار الزيادة على الأربع صحيح، وأما كلامهم في الزيادة على الثمان فغير مقبول، فقد ورد أنه صلى تسع
Before 'Asr, before 'Isha and after it. And it is disliked to increase supererogatory prayer beyond four (rak'ahs) with one taslim during the day, and beyond eight at night. 218 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya [1] His statement: 'Before 'Asr'; and after Dhuhr four, the four are beloved due to the hadith: 'Whoever guards four before Dhuhr and four after it, Allah will make him forbidden to the Fire,' reported by al-Tirmidhi and others. [2] His statement: 'And it is disliked' in the passive voice; i.e., it is disliked to perform supererogatory prayer in excess of four rak'ahs with one salutation in daytime supererogatory prayers, and in excess of eight at night. They reasoned this because it has not been transmitted nor reported that he (ﷺ) ever exceeded four rak'ahs with one salutation during the day or eight at night. Had it not been disliked, it would have been reported, if only occasionally, to teach permissibility. The apparent meaning of this reasoning is that the dislike is tanzihi (slightly disliked). Then, their statement regarding the denial of exceeding four is correct, but their statement regarding exceeding eight is unacceptable, for it has been transmitted that he prayed nine...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the recommended (mustahabb) four rak'ahs before 'Asr, before 'Isha, and after 'Isha, and the disliked (makruh) nature of exceeding four rak'ahs with one taslim during the day and eight at night in supererogatory prayers. The author first cites a hadith praising those who pray four before and after Dhuhr, establishing the recommendation of four rak'ahs in general. Then he addresses the ruling on exceeding these numbers: it is makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) to pray more than four rak'ahs with one taslim during the day and more than eight at night. The reasoning is that the Prophet (ﷺ) never did this, and if it were permissible, it would have been reported at least occasionally. The commentary notes that the dislike is tanzihi, not tahrimi. However, the commenter questions the ruling for the night, stating that there is evidence the Prophet (ﷺ) prayed nine rak'ahs at night (referring to the witr prayer with eight nafl? The text is cut off). Thus, the established Hanafi position is that daytime nafl should be limited to four rak'ahs per taslim, and nighttime nafl to eight, with the daytime ruling being stronger.

Key Terms
كُره (kuriḥa): Passive verb meaning 'it is disliked', indicating makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) in Hanafi terminology.
النفل (al-nafl): Supererogatory prayer, voluntary worship beyond the obligatory.
بتسليمة (bi-taslīma): With one taslim (salutation of peace), i.e., one unit of prayer ending with salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is not transmitted that the Prophet (ﷺ) did so
C) Because it invalidates the prayer
D) Because it is an innovation (bid'ah)

Key Takeaway
Daytime nafl should be limited to four rak'ahs per taslim, and nighttime to eight, due to the absence of prophetic practice, with the nighttime limit being disputed among Hanafi scholars.

Preference of Four Rak'ahs: Day vs Night in Hanafi Voluntary Prayers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on best number of rak'ahs in voluntary prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the best number of rak'ahs in voluntary prayers and explain why the Sahibayn differed from Abu Hanifa for night prayers.

Core Idea
Four rak'ahs are best by day; by night, two-by-two is stronger due to authentic hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the preferred number of rak'ahs in voluntary prayers. Abu Hanifa held that four consecutive rak'ahs are best in both day and night, citing the longer tahrima and greater reward. His disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, agreed for daytime but disagreed for night, relying on the hadith 'Night prayer is two by two.' The commentator declares their view more reliable. Understanding this dispute clarifies the operative sunnah for the practicing Hanafi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is being ruled upon? Voluntary prayers (nawafil). The dispute is about the best number of rak'ahs per taslim, not about validity.
Both sides agree all forms are valid.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa: 4 rak'ahs per taslim are best in both day and night. His dalil: longer tahrima means more continuous devotion, hence more reward.
Tahrima refers to the state of consecration initiated by the opening takbir.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agreed with Abu Hanifa for daytime, but for nighttime they preferred prayer in sets of two (muthanna muthanna). Their dalil: the hadith 'Night prayer is two by two' (Bukhari and Muslim).
They rely on a textual proof (nas) over the reasoning of longer tahrima.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: by day, 4 is best; by night, 2 by 2 is best, though praying 4 consecutively is still valid and permissible.
A person may still pray 4 at night but loses the sunnah of praying in twos.
5
Identify the tarjih
The commentator explicitly states that the Sahibayn's opinion is 'awthaq wa asahh' (more reliable and sound). Thus, for night prayers, the mufta bihi is to pray in twos.
This tarjih reflects later Hanafi practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأربع أفضلُ في المَلوَين

[1] قوله: أفضل؛ هذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه أشق وأدوم تحريمة، فيكون أكثر ثواباً، وبهذا قال صاحباه في النوافل النهارية، واستحبا في الليلية مثنى مثنى؛ لحديث: «صلاة الليل مثنى مثنى»، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم، وقولهما أوثق وأصح.
And four are best in the two times (day and night). [1] His saying: 'best' – this is according to Abu Hanifa, because it is more arduous and the tahrima lasts longer, so it has more reward. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also said this for daytime voluntary prayers, but they preferred for nighttime prayers to be in twos (i.e., two by two) due to the hadith: 'Night prayer is two by two,' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. And their statement is more reliable and sound.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states 'Four are best in the two times (day and night).' The commentator explains that this is the view of Abu Hanifa, who argued that four consecutive rak'ahs with one taslim are better because they involve a longer tahrima (opening takbir) and thus more continuous devotion, yielding greater reward. However, Abu Hanifa's two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, agreed with him for daytime voluntary prayers but differed for nighttime. They held that for night prayers, it is mustahabb (preferred) to pray in sets of two rak'ahs (muthanna muthanna), based on the well-known hadith 'Night prayer is two by two' (Bukhari and Muslim). The commentator then states that the opinion of the two disciples is stronger (awthaq wa asahh) in terms of evidence. Thus, the relied-upon practice: for day, 4 rak'ahs per taslim are best; for night, it is better to pray in twos, although praying 4 consecutively is still valid and permissible.

Key Terms
تحريمة (taḥrīma): The opening takbir (Allahu Akbar) that begins the prayer; it is the same for all rak'ahs in a single taslim.
مثنى مثنى (muthannā muthannā): Two by two; i.e., praying in sets of two rak'ahs, with a taslim after each pair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the best number for night voluntary prayers is two rak'ahs per taslim, following the Sahibayn's hadith-based opinion, while for day, four rak'ahs are best.

Recitation in Prayer: Fard in Two Rak'ahs
Definition of the fard of recitation in obligatory and voluntary prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in prayer and distinguish between fard and wajib in this context.

Core Idea
Recitation is fard in two rak'ahs of fard prayers and all rak'ahs of nafl and witr.

The matn states a concise ruling that carries deeper implications. The commentator breaks down the grammatical and legal reasoning behind this rule. In this lesson, we will parse the passage to understand why recitation is fard in only two rak'ahs of fard prayers but in every rak'ah of nafl and witr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling from the Matn
Read the matn: 'Recitation is fard in the two rak'ahs of the fard prayer, and in all of witr and nafl.' Note that it specifies 'two rak'ahs' for fard, not all rak'ahs.
The term 'fard' is used here for recitation.
2
Parse the Grammatical Construction of 'Fard'
The commentator explains that 'fard' can be read as passive or an infinitive. Understanding this helps identify the subject: the prescribed recitation amount.
Grammatical parsing aids legal precision.
3
Distinguish Fard from Wajib Regarding Which Rak'ahs
The fard applies to two rak'ahs generally. Designating the first two for recitation is a separate wajib obligation. Thus, if one recites in the first and third, the fard is fulfilled but the wajib is missed.
This distinction is a hallmark of Hanafi usul.
4
Understand the Reasoning for Nafl and Witr
In nafl, each pair (shaf') is an independent prayer, so recitation is fard in each rak'ah. Witr is treated similarly due to caution and its classification as a type of nafl.
The concept of shaf' is key to this reasoning.
5
Trace the Proof from the Quranic Verse and Qiyas
The obligator is the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara minhu' (73:20). A command does not imply repetition, so only two rak'ahs are required. The second rak'ah is included by qiyas (analogy) because it is similar to the first.
Qiyas is used to extend the ruling from one rak'ah to two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفَرْضُ القراءة في ركعتي الفرض، وكل الوتر والنفل

[2] قوله: وفرض؛ هو إمّا بصيغة المجهول فما بعده فاعله، وإما مصدر مضاف إلى ما بعده بمعنى القراءة المفروضة أو المقدار المفروض من القراءة، فهو مبتدأ، وخبره قوله: في ركعتي...الخ [3] قوله: ركعتي الفرض؛ في الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنّ الفرض في مطلق الركعتين، وإن كانتا أخريتين، وأما تعيين الأوليين للقراءة فواجب، وكلُّ الوتر والنفل - أي جميع ركعاتهما. أما كون القراءة فرضاً في جميع ركعات النفل؛ فلأن كل شفع منه صلاة على حدة، والقيام إلى الثالثة كتحريمة مبتدأة، وأما كونه كذلك في الوتر فللاحتياط للاختلاف في كونه واجباً أو سنة، وهو من أقسام النفل

وأما كون الفرض في ركعتي الفرض فقط، فلأنّ موجبه هو الأمر في قوله تعالى: فَاقْرَهُوا مَا تيسرينه (المزمل:20)، والأمر لا يقتضي التكرار، وإنّما لزمت في الثانية إلحاقاً لها بالركعة الأولى دلالة؛ لكونها مثلها. كذا في «الهداية» وغيرها.
And the recitation is fard in the two rak'ahs of the fard (prayer), and in all of witr and nafl. [2] His saying: 'and fard' – either it is in the passive voice and what follows is its agent, or it is a masdar (infinitive) annexed to what follows, meaning the prescribed recitation or the prescribed amount of recitation. So it is a mubtada', and its khabar is his saying: 'in the two rak'ahs...' [3] His saying: 'the two rak'ahs of fard' – the absolute expression indicates that the fard applies to the two rak'ahs in general, even if they are the last two. As for designating the first two for recitation, it is wajib. And 'all of witr and nafl' means all their rak'ahs. As for recitation being fard in all rak'ahs of nafl, because each pair (shaf') is a separate prayer, and standing for the third is like a new tahrima. As for it being so in witr, it is out of caution due to the difference of opinion on whether it is wajib or sunnah, and it is a type of nafl. As for recitation being fard only in the two rak'ahs of fard, because its obligator is the command in the verse: 'So recite what is easy of it' (73:20), and a command does not necessitate repetition. It became necessary in the second rak'ah only by way of linking it to the first by indication because it is like it. Thus in al-Hidayah and others.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on recitation (qira'a) in prayer. The matn states: 'Recitation is fard in the two rak'ahs of the fard prayer, and in all of witr and nafl.' The commentator explains the grammatical construction of 'fard' and then clarifies the details. Key points: (1) In obligatory (fard) prayers, recitation is fard only in two rak'ahs – the first two. Even if one prays four rak'ahs (Zuhr, Asr, etc.), the last two do not require recitation as fard; it is only wajib to recite in the first two. The term 'fard' here applies to the two rak'ahs generally, without specifying which two, but it is wajib to make them the first two. (2) In nafl (voluntary) prayers and witr, recitation is fard in every rak'ah because each pair (two rak'ahs) is considered an independent prayer, and beginning the third rak'ah is like a new tahrima. For witr, caution is exercised because of differing views on its status (wajib or sunnah), but it is treated as nafl. (3) The proof for recitation being fard only in two rak'ahs of fard prayers is the Quranic verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara minhu' (So recite what is easy of it). The command (amr) does not imply repetition; it becomes necessary in the second rak'ah only by analogy (qiyas) because it is similar to the first. Thus, the Hanafi position: recitation is fard in two rak'ahs of fard prayers (the first two) and in all rak'ahs of nafl and witr.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligatory element whose omission invalidates the prayer. In this context, recitation is fard in specified rak'ahs.
شفع (shaf'): A pair of rak'ahs; in nafl, each pair is an independent prayer unit with its own taslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All four
B) Only the first two
C) Only the last two
D) None, it is only sunnah

Key Takeaway
Recitation is fard only in two rak'ahs of fard prayers (the first two) and in all rak'ahs of nafl and witr; the designation of the first two for recitation is wajib.

Best Form of Supererogatory Prayers and Obligatory Recitation
Ruling on best form of nafl and obligation of recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on the best form of nafl prayer and EXPLAIN where recitation is obligatory, differentiating between fard, witr, and nafl.

Core Idea
Recitation is fard only in two rak'ahs of fard but in every rak'ah of witr and nafl; night nafl is best prayed two by two.

This passage addresses two important rulings: the most rewarding way to perform supererogatory prayers, and the obligation of recitation (qira'ah) in different types of prayers. Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two senior disciples regarding the best form of night nafl. Additionally, the passage clarifies the scope of the Quranic command to recite, which has practical implications for every prayer. By understanding these rulings, you will be able to apply the correct method and avoid common errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The matn presents two distinct rulings: (1) the best number of rak'ahs for supererogatory prayers during day and night, and (2) the obligation of recitation in fard, witr, and nafl. Separate these in your mind.
The first ruling is about form and reward; the second is about a required element.
2
Analyze the best form dispute
Examine Abu Hanifa's view (4 rak'ahs with one salam are best) and the Sahibayn's view (2 by 2 at night). Identify their proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on longer tahrimah and more hardship; the Sahibayn rely on the hadith 'Night prayer is two by two.'
The commentator states that the Sahibayn's view is 'more sound and authentic'.
3
Analyze the recitation ruling
The matn says recitation is fard in the first two rak'ahs of fard and in all rak'ahs of witr and nafl. Note the reasoning: in fard, the Quranic command does not imply repetition; in nafl, each pair is an independent prayer. In witr, caution applies due to differing opinions on its status.
The last two rak'ahs of fard are silent; recitation is required there but as a wajib not fard? The passage says fard in two rak'ahs.
4
Examine the dalil and its interpretation
The primary dalil for recitation obligation is Quran 73:20 'faqra'u ma tayassara minhu'. The Hanafis interpret the imperative as indicating obligation but not repetition. Thus it applies to only two rak'ahs in fard. Additional evidence (qiyas) extends it to all rak'ahs of nafl.
Understand that the command is general but restricted by other principles.
5
Summarize practical outcomes
Apply the rulings: For night nafl, the mufta bihi is to pray two by two. For any fard prayer, recite in the first two rak'ahs only. For witr and all nafl, recite in every rak'ah.
Remember: the best form for day nafl is four rak'ahs with one salam according to all.

The Book of Prayer - Section on Witt and Supererogatory Prayers 219. And four (rak'ahs) are best during the day and night. And recitation is obligatory in the two rak'ahs of the obligatory prayer, and in all of the witt and supererogatory prayer. Commentary
This passage presents two related rulings from the matn. First, regarding the best form of supererogatory prayers: Abu Hanifa holds that four rak'ahs with one salam are best both day and night, as they are more arduous and involve a longer state of ihrām (tahrimah), thus more reward. His two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, agree for daytime nafl but prefer two rak'ahs at night, relying on the hadith 'Night prayer is two by two'. The author notes that the disciples' view is 'more sound and authentic'. Second, the ruling on obligatory recitation: In obligatory prayers (fard), recitation (qira'ah) is obligatory only in the first two rak'ahs; in witt and nafl, it is obligatory in every rak'ah. The difference arises because the command in the Qur'an (73:20) does not imply repetition, so in fard it is limited to two rak'ahs. In nafl, each pair is considered an independent prayer, so recitation is required in each rak'ah. In witt, caution dictates obligation due to scholarly disagreement on its status. The commentary clarifies that in fard, the obligation applies to any two rak'ahs (not necessarily the first two), but the specification of the first two for recitation is wajib (necessary), not fard.

This is a ruling_with_proof type, as it cites the Qur'anic verse and the hadith, along with rational reasoning (qiyas). The position of the Hanafi school is that recitation is fard in the first two rak'ahs of fard and in all rak'ahs of witt and nafl. Within the school, there is an intra-madhhab dispute regarding night prayers (Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn), with the mufta bihi being the Sahibayn's view (two by two).

المَلوان (al-malawān): The two periods: day and night.
تحريمة (taḥrīmah): The opening takbir that initiates the prayer; here referring to the state of being in prayer.
شفع (shaf'): A pair of rak'ahs; each two rak'ahs form a unit in nafl.

Key Takeaway

Obligation to Complete a Voluntary Prayer Once Started
Establishing the duty to finish a voluntary prayer if intentionally begun, with exceptions and consequences of nullification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on completing a voluntary prayer once started and explain the proof from Quran, hadith, and qiyas, including the exception for mistaken intention and the rule for partial nullification.

Core Idea
A nafl prayer becomes wajib once started; nullifying it requires qada of the part broken.

Scholars agree that obligatory acts must be completed, but what about voluntary ones? The Hanafi school holds that once you intentionally begin a nafl prayer, it becomes binding—you cannot simply walk away. This lesson unpacks the textual proof from the Quran (47:33), the analogy to voluntary fasting, and the practical implications for four-rak'ah prayers. You will also learn the one case where the obligation drops: if you mistakenly thought the prayer was fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is starting a nafl prayer intentionally. The ruling is that completion becomes wajib (obligatory).
This applies even during prohibited times (sunrise/sunset), though starting then is sinful.
2
Locate the primary proof
The Quranic verse 47:33: 'Wa la tubtilu a'malakum' (Do not invalidate your deeds). The prohibition implies that once a worship is begun, it must be completed.
The verse is general, but Hanafi usul applies it as evidence for binding completion.
3
Examine the qiyas to voluntary fasting
A hadith reports that the Prophet ordered a person who broke a voluntary fast to make it up. Prayer is analogous to fasting, indeed stronger, so the same rule applies.
The hadith is narrated by multiple chains, strengthening its authority.
4
Apply the rule to a four-rak'ah nafl
If one nullifies part of a four-rak'ah nafl, he must make up only the pair (shaf') he nullified, because each two rak'ahs is an independent unit.
For example, breaking the second pair requires qada of two rak'ahs, not four.
5
Identify the exception
If a person starts a prayer thinking it is obligatory (e.g., believing he missed Fajr), and then learns it is not, no completion or qada is required.
This is because he did not intend to start a nafl; the intention was mistaken.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولزم إتمام نفل شرع فيه قصداً ولو عند الطلوع والغروب وقضى ركعتين لو نقض الشفع الأول أو الثاني [1] قوله : ولزم ...الخ ; وجهه : أنه لما شرع فيه وأدى قدراً منه وقع ما أدى قربة، بدليل أنه لو مات حينئذ يثاب عليه، فيلزم عليه الإتمام ; لقوله تعالى : ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم، وإذا ثبت لزوم الإتمام فلو أفسده يجب عليه القضاء. وقد ورد ذلك في صوم التطوع أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر من أفسده بالقضاء، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن حبان والطبراني والبزار وغيرهم بطرق متعددة يقوي بعضها بعضاً، ومن المعلوم أن الصلاة نظير الصوم، بل أعلى منه. [2] قوله : لا يجب القضاء ; لأنه ما شرع فيه إلا على ظن أن الفرض عليه، وقد تبين بطلانه، ولم يلزمه مطلقاً حتى يلزمه إتمامه. [3] قوله : ولو عند ; أي لو كان الشروع في النفل في الأوقات التي نهي عن الصلاة فيها ; لأنه صار إنما بالتزامه وإن لزم عليه الإثم لمخالفته للنهي.
And it is obligatory to complete a supererogatory (prayer) that one has started intentionally, even at (the times of) sunrise and sunset, and he makes up two rak'ahs if he breaks the first or second pair. [1] His saying: 'And it is obligatory...' – its reasoning: since he started it and performed some of it, what he performed occurred as an act of worship, evidenced by the fact that if he died then he would be rewarded for it, so completion becomes binding due to His saying: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33). And when the binding of completion is established, then if he invalidates it, he must make it up. This has been reported concerning voluntary fasting: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered the one who invalidated it to make it up. Narrated by Abu Dawud, at-Tirmidhi, an-Nasa'i, Ibn Hibban, at-Tabarani, al-Bazzar and others through multiple chains that strengthen each other. And it is well known that prayer is analogous to fasting, indeed superior to it. [2] His saying: 'the making up is not obligatory' – because he only started it under the assumption that it was obligatory upon him, and that (assumption) has become invalid, and it (the prayer) was not binding on him absolutely such that its completion would be binding. [3] His saying: 'even at' – meaning even if the start of the supererogatory was during the times when prayer is prohibited; because he became committed to it, although he incurs sin for violating the prohibition.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that once a person intentionally begins a voluntary prayer (nafl), it becomes binding upon him to complete it. The evidence is both rational and scriptural: rationally, the part already performed is an act of worship (as shown by the reward if one dies during it), and the Qur'an (47:33) prohibits nullifying good deeds, implying completion. Scripturally, the hadith about voluntary fasting affirms that if one breaks it, he must make it up, and prayer is analogous (qiyas) to fasting, even stronger. The rule applies even if the prayer is started during prohibited times (e.g., at sunrise or sunset), though the person sins for starting at such a time. However, if one mistakenly starts a prayer thinking it is obligatory (e.g., thinking he missed Fajr), then realizes it is not, he is not obliged to complete it or to make it up. The passage also addresses nullifying a four-rak'ah nafl: if one breaks it during the first or second pair, he must make up only the pair he nullified (i.e., two rak'ahs), not the entire four. This is because each pair is considered an independent unit.

This is a ruling_with_proof type, using the Quranic verse and the hadith on fasting as proofs, along with qiyas. The Hanafi position is clear: it is wajib to complete a voluntarily started prayer, and if nullified, qada is wajib. The exception for mistaken intention is not a true exception to the rule, as the prayer was not genuinely intended as nafl.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up an obligatory or necessary act of worship after its time has passed or after it was nullified.
نقض (naqd): To nullify or break a prayer, e.g., by invalidating actions like talking, eating, etc.
الشفع الأول أو الثاني (al-shaf' al-awwal aw al-thānī): The first two rak'ahs or the second two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah nafl prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone starts a four-rak'ah voluntary prayer and nullifies the second pair (third and fourth rak'ahs), what must they do according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Nothing, because the prayer is voluntary
B) Make up the entire prayer (four rak'ahs)
C) Make up only the second pair (two rak'ahs)
D) Repeat the entire prayer with a different intention

Key Takeaway
A nafl prayer, once started intentionally, becomes wajib to complete; nullification requires qada of the portion nullified, and each pair in a four-rak'ah prayer is an independent unit.

Obligation to Complete Voluntary Prayer Once Begun
Explaining the ruling of completing nafl prayer and nullification consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on completing a nafl prayer and apply the principle of each shaf' independence to determine the required qada' when a multi-rak'ah nafl is nullified.

Core Idea
A nafl prayer becomes wajib upon intentional commencement; each pair (shaf') is an independent prayer unit.

This passage clarifies when a voluntary prayer becomes obligatory to complete and what happens if one nullifies it. The key Hanafi principle is that each pair of rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah nafl is a separate prayer unit. This has significant practical consequences for making up broken prayers, especially in distinguishing the relied-upon majority view from that of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (commencing a nafl prayer) and the initial hukm (wajib to complete). Note the exclusion for mistaken commencement.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: Quran 47:33 'Do not invalidate your deeds' and the hadith on qada' for a broken voluntary fast, analogized to prayer.
The hadith is ahad but supported by multiple chains.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the imperative 'la tubtilu' indicates obligation, and the analogy (qiyas) from fasting to prayer is stronger.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold each shaf' is independent; Abu Yusuf holds the entire four-rak'ah intention binds the whole prayer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon: only the nullified shaf' requires qada'.
Tarjih supported by the principle that the first shaf' is complete upon sitting.
